then consciously, but sooner or later he will take his position — more or less 
active, decisive or vacillating. For this reason, under conditions of class con- 
frontation he does not simply ‘‘show himself” but takes his place on one 
side or the other of the barricade. Something else becomes evident, specifical- 
ly, that at every turn of his life’s way he must free himself of something, 
confirm something in himself, and he must do all this and not simply “‘sub- 
mit to the effect of the environment.” 
Finally, along this line there takes place still another change, which also 
changes the very “‘mechanism”’ that forms personality. Earlier I spoke about 
the ever-widening activity that actually exists for the subject. But it exists 
also within time — in the form of his past and in the form of the future he 
sees before him. Of course, primarily we have in mind the first thing — the 
subject’s individual experience, the function of which appears to be, as it 
were, his personality. And this again resurrects the formula about personality 
as a product of innate properties and acquisition of experience. At earlier 
stages of development this formula can still seem credible, especially if it is 
not simplified and if all the complexity of the mechanisms that go into form- 
ing experience are considered. Under conditions of the hierarchization of 
motives, however, it continuously loses its meaning and at the level of 
personality it seems to topple. 
The fact is that at this level past impressions, experiences, and actual ac- 
tions of the subject do not in any way appear to him as dormant layers of 
his experience. They are the subject of his relations and his actions and for 
132 
ACTIVITY AND PERSONALITY 
that reason their contribution is changed into personality. One thing in the 
past dies, loses its sense, and is converted into a simple condition and means 
of his activity: the developed aptitudes, skills, and stereotypes of behavior; 
everything else appears to the subject in a completely new light and acquires 
a new meaning, which he had not perceived before; finally, something from 
the past may be actively rejected by the subject and psychologically ceases 
to exist for him although it remains in the compendium of his memory. These 
changes take place gradually, but they may be concentrated and may com- 
prise moral breaks. The resulting reevaluation of the past that is established 
in life leads to man’s casting off from himself the burden of his biography. 
Does this not in itself indicate that the contributions of past experience to 
personality were dependent on personality itself and became its function? 
This seems to be possible because of the new internal movement that has 
arisen in the system of individual consciousness, which I have figuratively 
called a movement “along the vertical.’’ But one must not think that major 
changes in personality in the past were produced by consciousness; con- 
sciousness does not produce them but simply mediates them; they are 
produced by the actions of the subject, sometimes even external actions — 
breaks of former contacts, a change in profession, a practical entering into 
new circumstances. This was beautifully described by Makarenko: Old cloth- 
ing worn by orphans in an orphanage is publicly burned by them on a 
bonfire. 
Despite its prevalence, the consideration of personality as a product of 
the biography of man is unsatisfactory, confirming as it does the fatalistic 
understanding of his fate (A citizen thinks thus: The child stole; therefore 
he will be a thief!). This view, of course, allows the possibility of changing 
something in man, but only at the price of external interference, the force 
of which outweighs the accumulation of his experience. This is a concep- 
tion of the primacy of punishment and not repentance, reward and not 
action that it rewards. The main psychological fact is overlooked, specifically, 
that man enters into relations with his past, which enters variously into his 
present — into the memory of his personality. Tolstoy advised: Notice what 
you remember and what you do not remember; by these signs you will 
recognize yourself.4 
This view is incorrect also because an expansion of activity for man takes 
place not only in the direction of the past but also in the direction of the 
future. Just like the past, the future is also present in the personality. The 
life perspective opening before man is not simply a product of a “reflection 
left behind” but also its property. In this lies the strength and the truth of 
what Makarenko wrote about the developmental nurturing significance of 
close perspectives and of more distant perspectives. This is true also for 
“LN. Tolstoy, Complete Collected Works, Vol. 54, Moscow, 1935, p. 31. 
FORMATION OF PERSONALITY 
133 
adults. The following is a parable that I heard at one time from an old stable- 
man in the Urals: When a horse on a difficult road begins to stumble, then it 
is necessary not to whip it but to raise its head higher so that it might see 
farther ahead. 
A personality is created by objective circumstances but in no other way 
than through the whole aggregate of the activity that realizes its relations 
with the world. The features of the activity also form that which determines 
the type of personality. Although questions of differential psychology are 
not a part of the problem here, the analysis of forming a personality none- 
theless leads to the problem of a general approach to investigating these 
questions. 
The first basis of personality that no differential-psychological concep- 
tion can ignore is the riches of the connections of the individual with the 
world. These riches also distinguish a man whose life encompasses a wide 
circle of various activities from that Berlin teacher whose “world stretches 
from Moabite to Kyonenik and who is locked fast behind the Hamburg 
gates, his relationships to that world being reduced to a minimum by his 
pitiable position in life.’’*? It is understood that we are speaking about real 
relationships and not about relationships alienated from man, which resist 
him or subordinate him to themselves. Psychologically, we express these 
real relationships through an understanding of activity, its sense-forming 
motives, and not in the language of stimuli and completed operations. It 
must be added here that activities forming the basis of personality include 
in themselves theoretical activities also, and that in the course of develop- 
ment their circle can not only expand but also contract; in empirical psy- 
chology this is called “‘a contraction of interests.’”’ Some people do not 
notice this contraction; others, like Darwin, complain about it as a mis- 
fortune.* 
The differences that exist here are not just quantitative, expressing the 
measure of the extent to which the world opens before man in space and 
time, in his future. Behind them lie the differences in content of these ob- 
jective and social relationships that are mandated by the objective condi- 
tions of the epoch, nation, and class. For this reason the approach to the 
typology of personalities, even if it considers only one such parameter, in 
current terminology, cannot but be concrete—historical. But psychological 
analysis does not stop at this, for the connections of personality with the 
world either may be poorer than those that set the objective conditions or 
may substantially surpass them. 
A second and more important parameter of personality is the degree to 
which activities and their motives are arranged hierarchically. This degree 
43K. Marx and F. Engels, Works, Vol. 3, p. 253. 
44 Charles Darwin, Notes on the Development of My Mind and Character, Autobiography, Moscow, 
1957, pp. 147-148. 
134 
ACTIVITY AND PERSONALITY 
may be very different regardless of whether the personality base forming 
the subject’s connections with the environment is narrow or broad. The 
hierarchies of motives exist always atall levels of development. It is these 
motives that form relatively independent units of the life of the personality, 
and they may be smaller or larger, split one from another or within a single 
motivational sphere. Splitting of these units of life that are hierarchically 
arranged within themselves creates the psychological makeup of a person 
living fragmentarily, first in one “field,” then in another. On the other hand, 
a higher degree of hierarchization of motives is expressed in the fact that 
man seems to measure his actions against his main motives-goals and then 
finds that some of these are in direct contradiction to a given motive, and 
others directly respond to it, and still others lead away from it. 
When the principal motive that stimulates man is under consideration, 
then usually we are speaking about life goal. Is this motive, however, always 
adequately disclosed to consciousness? This question cannot be answered 
lightly because its perception in the form of understanding the idea occurs 
not of itself but in that movement of individual perception through which 
alone the subject is capable of interpreting what is internal to him through 
a system of assimilated meanings or concepts. We have already talked about 
this and about the struggle that is waged in society for the consciousness 
of man. 
Units of meaning of life may gather as if into one stream, but this is a 
figurative characterization. The question that remains most important is 
which place is occupied by that point in extensive space that constitutes the 
real, although not always apparent to the individual, genuine reality. The 
whole life of the Covetous Knight was directed to one goal: acquiring the 
“power of gold.’ This purpose was attained (“Who knows how many bitter 
abstentions, restrained passions, heavy thoughts, days of worry, sleepless 
nights all of this cost?’’), but life ended in nothing and the goal seemed 
senseless. Pushkin ends the tragedy of the Covetous Knight with the words, 
“A dreadful age! dreadful hearts!” 
A different personality with a different fate is created when the principal 
motive-goal is elevated to a truly human level and does not weaken man but 
merges his life with the life of people, with their good. Depending on the 
circumstances that are the fate of man, such life motives may acquire very 
different content and different objective significance, but only they are 
capable of creating an internal psychological justification for his existence, 
which comprises the sense and happiness of life. The summit on this road is 
man having become, in the words of Gorki, a man of Man. 
Here we approach the most complex parameter of personality: the general 
type of its structure. The motivational sphere of man, even in its highest 
development, never resembles a stiff pyramid. It may be displaced, eccentric 
with respect to the actual space of historical reality, and then we describe it 
FORMATION OF PERSONALITY 
135 
as a one-sided personality. It may, on the other hand, develop as a many- 
sided personality including a wide circle of relationships. But in the one case 
as well as in the other it necessarily reflects objective nonconformity of these 
relationships, the contradictions between them, and the shift of the place 
they occupy in it. 
The structure of personality represents in itself a relatively stable con- 
figuration of principal motivational lines arranged hierarchically within itself. 
We are speaking here about the fact that “‘direction of personality” is in- 
completely described, incompletely because even in the presence of a distinct 
predominant line of life in a man, itstill cannot be the only line. Serving the 
select goal or ideal does not at all exclude nor extinguish other life relation- 
ships of man, which in their turn form sense-forming motives. Figuratively 
speaking, the motivational sphere of personality always appears multi- 
storied, just like that objective system of axiological concepts that charac- 
terizes the ideology of a given society, a given class or social stratum that is 
communized and assimilated (or rejected) by man. 
Internal relationships of main motivational lines in the aggregate activity 
of man form as if a general “‘psychological profile’ of personality. Sometimes 
it takes on the configuration of a flatness devoid of real summits; then what 
is small in life man takes for something large, and the large things he does not 
see at all. Such poverty of personality may under certain social conditions 
be combined with a satisfaction of a fairly wide circle of everyday needs. 
In this, incidentally, lies that psychological threat that modern consumer 
society poses to the personality of man. 
A different structure of psychological profile of personality is created by 
parallelism of life motives, often combined with the rise of imaginary peaks 
formed only by “familiar motives’? — stereotypes of ideals, devoid of person- 
al sense. Such a structure, however, is transient: From the beginning the 
parallelism of lines of various life relationships enters subsequently into 
internal connections. This occurs inevitably, but not of itself; it is a result 
of the internal work about which I spoke earlier and which appears in the 
form of a specific movement of consciousness. 
Multifaceted relationships into which man enters with reality are objec- 
tively contradictory. Their contradictions engender conflicts that under 
certain circumstances are fixed and enter into the structure of personality. 
Thus a historically arising separation of internal theoretical activity not only 
gives rise to a one-sided development of personality but may lead to psy- 
chological disorders, to splitting of personality into two spheres strange to 
each other — the sphere of its appearance in real life and the sphere of its 
appearance in the life that exists only as an illusion, only in autistic thought. 
It is impossible to describe such a psychological disturbance more penetrat- 
ingly than did Dostoyevsky; from a wretched existence filled with senseless 
matters, his hero escapes into a life of the imagination, into dreams; before 
136 
ACTIVITY AND PERSONALITY 
us there are as if two personalities, one, the personality of a man who is 
humiliatingly cowardly, an eccentric who shuts himself off in his den, the 
other, aromantic and even a heroic personality open to all the joys of life. 
And this is the life of one and the same man; for that reason inevitably 
there comes a moment when the dreams are dissipated and years of gloomy 
solitude, melancholy, and despondency follow. 
The personality of the hero of ‘White Nights”’ is also a special, even 
unique phenomenon. But through this uniqueness there is evident a general 
psychological truth. This truth is that the structure of personality devolves 
neither to the riches of connections between man and the world nor to the 
degree to which they are arranged in hierarchies, that their characterization 
lies rather in the correlation of the various systems developed by the life 
relationships that engender conflict among them. Sometimes this conflict 
takes place in externally imperceptible, ordinarily dramatic forms so to speak, 
and does not disturb the harmony of the personality or its development; after 
all, a harmonious personality is not at all a personality that does not know 
any kind of an internal struggle. Sometimes, however, this internal struggle 
becomes the main thing that determines the whole makeup of the man; such 
is the structure of the tragic personality. 
Thus theoretical analysis allows an isolation of at least three basic param- 
eters of personality: the extent of the connections of man with the world, 
the degree to which they are arranged in hierarchies, and their general struc- 
ture. Of course, these parameters do not give the differential psychological 
typology; they can only serve as a skeletal plan, which must still be filled 
with a living concrete—historical content. But this is a problem for special 
investigation. Will there not occur, however, under these circumstances a 
substitution of sociological psychology, will not the “psychological” in 
personality be lost? 
This question arises because the approach about which we are speaking 
differs from the usual anthropologist (or cultural—anthropologistic) approach 
to the psychology of personality, which considers personality as an individual 
having psychophysiological and psychological traits that are changed in the 
process of his adaptation to the social environment. Our analysis, on the 
contrary, requires consideration of personality as a new quality engendered 
by the movement of the systems of objective social relations into which his 
activity is drawn. Personality thus no longer seems to be the result of a direct 
layering of external influences; it appears as something that man makes of 
himself, confirming his human life. He confirms it in everyday affairs and 
contacts, as well as in people to whom he gives some part of himself on the 
barricades of class struggles, as well as on the fields of battle for his country, 
and at times he consciously confirms it even at the price of his physical life. 
As far as such psychological “substructures of personality” as tempera- 
ment, needs and inclinations, emotional experiences and interests, aims, 
FORMATION OF PERSONALITY 
137 
habits and customs, moral traits, etc., are concerned, it is understood that 
they do not in the least disappear. They are only evident in different ways: 
either as conditions or in their origins and transformations, in changes of 
their place in personality, which take place in the process of their develop- 
ment. 
Thus the characteristics of the nervous system undoubtedly represent 
individual and at the same time quite stable traits; these traits, however, 
do not in any way form human personality. In his actions man consciously 
or unconsciously deals with the traits of his constitution just as he deals 
with external conditions of his actions and with the means he has for ac- 
complishing them. Characterizing man as a natural being, the traits, however, 
cannot play the role of forces that determine the motivation of activity 
and goal formation that are forming in him. The only real problem — al- 
though it arises secondarily here — the problem of the psychology of 
personality, is a problem of the formation of actions of the subject directed 
toward his own innate or acquired characteristics, which do not directly 
enter into the psychological characterization of his personality sphere. 
Even less can the factors or ““modes”’ of personality such as needs and 
purposes be considered as substructures. They appear only as abstracted 
from the activity of the subject in which their metamorphoses take place; 
but it is not these metamorphoses that create personality; on the contrary, 
they themselves are engendered by the movement of the development of 
personality. This movement is subject to the same formula that describes 
the transformation of human needs. It begins from the subject’s acting in 
order to sustain his existence; it leads to the subject’s sustaining his exis- 
tence in order to act, to carry out the business of his life, to accomplish his 
human purpose. This reversal, concluding the stage of establishing of per- 
sonality, also discloses the unlimited perspectives for its development. 
Object-material ‘needs for oneself’? having been satisfied, their satisfac- 
tion leads to their being reduced to the level of conditions of life, which are 
noticed the less by man the more habitual they become. For this reason 
personality cannot develop within the framework of need; its development 
necessarily presupposes a displacement of needs by creation, which alone 
does not know limits. 
Must this be emphasized? Of course it must, because the naive and, in 
essence, vestigial sense sometimes represents a transition to the principle, 
“according to need,” almost as a transition to the superprosperous consumer 
society. Lost from view here is the fact that it is necessary also to go through 
a transformation of material consumption, that the possibility for everyone 
to satisfy these needs does away with the intrinsic value of things that satisfy 
them and eliminates that unnatural function that they fulfill in private owner- 
ship society — a function of confirming through them man himself, his own 
“prestige.” 
138 
ACTIVITY AND PERSONALITY 
The last theoretical question I will consider is the question of perceiving 
oneself as a personality. In psychology it is posed as a question of self-con- 
sciousness, a question of the process of its development. There are a great 
number of works dedicated to an investigation of this process. They contain 
detailed data characterizing the stages of formation in the ontogenesis of 
representations about oneself. We are speaking about the formation of the 
so-called body plan, the potentials for localizing one’s interior receptive 
sensations, about the development of cognition of one’s external aspect — 
recognizing oneself in a mirror or in a photograph. Carefully observed is the 
process of the development in children of the evaluation of others and of 
themselves in which physical characteristics are isolated first and then psy- 
chological and moral characteristics are added to these. A change that 
proceeds parallel to this is that partial characterization of others and oneself 
yields to characterization that is more complete, one that encompasses man 
as a whole and his essential distinguishing traits. Such is the empirical picture 
of the development of self-recognition, of the recognition of one’s own in- 
dividual traits, properties, and potentials. Does this picture, however, answer 
the question about the development of self-consciousness, of the perception 
of the “1?” 
Yes, if we understand self-perception only as knowing about oneself. Like 
all cognition, self-cognition begins with isolating external superficial prop- 
erties and is the result of comparison, analysis, and generalization, of isolat- 
ing the essential. But individual consciousness is not only knowing, it is 
not only a system of acquired knowledge or concepts. Its property is an 
internal movement that reflects the movement of the real life of the subject 
itself, which it mediates; we have already seen that only in this movement 
does knowledge find its relevance to the objective world and its efficacy. The 
matter is also the same when the object of consciousness is the traits, features 
and actions or conditions of the subject himself; in this case it is also neces- 
sary to distinguish between knowledge about oneself and knowing oneself. 
Knowledge, representations about oneself, begins to accumulate even in 
early childhood; in imperceptible forms it evidently exists also in higher 
animals. Self-knowledge, perception of one’s “‘I,”’ is another matter. It is 
the result, the product, of the formation of man as a personality. Represent- 
ing in itself the phenomenological conversion of forms of actual relation- 
ships of the personality and its directness, it appears as their cause and subject. 
The psychological problem of the “I” arises as soon as we pose the ques- 
tion: To what kind of reality is everything that we know about ourselves 
related, and does everything that we know about ourselves relate to this 
reality.? How is it that in one reality I find my “I” and in another I lose it 
(we even say, 
“I am not myself. 
. .”)? The noncorrespondence of “I” and that 
which the subject represents as an object of his own knowledge of himself 
is psychologically evident. In addition, psychology originating from an 
> 
FORMATION OF PERSONALITY 
139 
organistic position cannot give a scientific explanation of this noncoincidence. 
If the problem of “I” is proposed in it, then it is only in the form of a state- 
ment of existence of a special instance within personality — a small man 
within the heart who at the proper moment “pulls on the strings.” It is 
understood that rejecting the possibility of ascribing substantially to this 
special instance, psychology ends in evading the problem, in dissipating the 
“T” in the structure of personality, and its interactions with the surrounding 
world. Nevertheless, it still remains, showing itself now in the form of a 
drive to penetrate into the world, into the need to “‘actualize oneself” that 
is within the individual.*5 
Thus the problem of self-consciousness of the personality, perception of 
the “I,” remains unresolved in psychology. And this is not in any way an 
imaginary problem; on the contrary, it is a problem of great vital significance 
crowning the psychology of personality. 
V.I. Lenin wrote about what distinguishes “simply a slave’”’ from a slave 
who is reconciled to his position and from a slave who has rebelled.*® This 
difference lies not in knowing one’s own individual traits but in perceiving 
oneself in a system of social relations. Perceiving one’s “I”? does not mean 
anything else. 
We have become accustomed to thinking that man represents a center in 
which are focused external influences and from which spread lines of his 
connections, his interactions with the external world, that this center, given 
consciousness, is really this “‘I.’’ But this is not at all the way the matter 
stands. We have seen that multifaceted activities of the subject are intertwined 
one with another and connected in knots by objective relationships, social 
in their nature, into which he necessarily enters. These knots, their hierarchies, 
also form that secret ‘‘center of personality,’’ which we call the “I’’; in other 
words, this center lies not in the individual, not under the surface of his 
skin, but in his being. 
Thus the analysis of activity and consciousness unavoidably leads to a 
rejection of the traditional, for empirical psychology, egocentric, “Ptole- 
maic”’ understanding of man in favor of the “Copernican,” which considers 
the human “I” as incorporated into a general system of interconnections of 
people in society. It is only necessary to emphasize here that inclusion in the 
system does not at all mean being dissolved in it but, on the contrary, means 
finding and disclosing in it the force of one’s action. 
In our psychological literature the words of Marx are often quoted: Man 
is not born a Fichtean philosopher, man looks at another man as if into a 
mirror and only by behaving toward him as he would behave toward himself 
does he begin to behave toward himself as to a man. These words are often 
45]. Nuttin, La Structure de la Personnalité, Paris, 1925, p. 234. 
46V_ I. Lenin, Complete Collected Works, Vol. 16, p. 40. 
140 
ACTIVITY AND PERSONALITY 
understood only in the sense that man forms his image according to the 
image of another man. But in these words is expressed a much deeper mean- 
ing. In order to understand this, it is sufficient to reestablish their context. 
“In certain relations,” begins Marx in the comment cited, “man resembles 
a commodity.” What are these relationships? Evidently they are those rela- 
tionships discussed in the text that accompanies the quoted comment. These 
are the cost relations of commodities. These relationships are based on the 
fact that the natural body of one commodity becomes the form and reflects 
the cost of another commodity, i.e., they are the relationships of such super- 
ficial quality that the body of the commodity is never penetrated. Marx 
ends this note thus: “In addition even Paul as such, in all of his Pauline 
physicality, becomes for him a form of disclosure of the genus “man” 
(author’s emphasis, A. L.).’’47 But, for Marx, man as a generic being is not 
the biological species Homo sapiens but a human society. In him, in his 
personified forms, man also sees himself as a man. 
The problem of the human “‘I’’ belongs to a number of problems that 
have been overlooked by scientific psychological analysis. Access to it is 
closed by many false representations compiled in psychology at the empirical 
level of the investigation of personality. At this level personality inevitably 
appears as an individual complicated but not transformed by society, that is, 
finding in it new systemic properties. But exactly in these, his “pretersensual”’ 
properties, he embodies a subject for psychological science. 
“7K. Marx and F. Engels, Works, Vol. 235Dv Oo: 
Conclusion 
Although I call these pages the conclusion, the task here is not to sum up 
the work but rather to note future perspectives. In my view they appear as 
an investigation of those transitions that may be called interlevel transitions. 
With no difficulty we isolate various levels of the study of man: the biolog- 
ical level on which he appears as a physical, natural being, the psychological 
level on which he appears as a subject of life activity, and finally, the social 
level on which he appears as realizing objective social relations, the social— 
historical process. The existence of these levels poses a problem about in- 
ternal relationships that connect the psychological level with the biological 
and the social. 
Although this problem has confronted psychology for a long time, even 
now it cannot be considered resolved. The difficulty is that for a scientific 
solution a preliminary abstraction is required of those specific interactions 
and connections of the subject that engender the psychic reflection of reality 
in the human brain. The category of activity actually contains this abstrac- 
tion, and this, it is understood, not only does not destroy the wholeness of 
the concrete subject as we see him at work, in his family, or even in our 
laboratories, but, on the contrary, returns him to psychology. 
Returning the whole man to psychology, however, may be accomplished 
only on the basis of a special investigation of the intertransitions of certain 
levels into others, which occurs in the course of development. Such investiga- 
tion must reject the idea of considering these levels as superimposed one on 
another, and even more strongly that of reducing one level to another. The 
obviousness of this is particularly evident in the study of ontogenesis. If, in 
the initial steps of the child’s psychological development, his biological adapta- 
tions (which make a decisive contribution to establishing his perceptions and 
emotions) appear at the first plane, then subsequently these adaptations are 
transformed. This of course does not mean that they simply stop functioning; 
141 
142 
CONCLUSION 
it means something else, specifically that they begin to realize another higher 
level of activity on which the amount they contribute at each given stage of 
development depends. Our dual task consists, therefore, of investigating the 
possibility (or limitation) that they embody. In ontogenetic development 
this problem recurs constantly, sometimes in a very sharp form as it does, let 
us say, in the puberty period when biological changes occur, which from the 
very beginning have an already transformed expression psychologically, and 
when the whole question is what kind of expressions these will be. 
But let us put aside the question of development psychology. The whole 
principle on which interlevel relations depend consists of the fact that the 
available higher level always becomes dominant, but it cannot be realized 
except with the help of lower-lying levels and is thus dependent on them. 
The problem of interlevel investigations, then, is studying the multifaceted 
forms of these realizations due to which the processes of the higher level are 
not only concretized but also individualized. 
The main thing that must not be lost from view is that in interlevel in- 
vestigations we have to do not with something that is only one-sided but 
with something that is two-sided and that has a movement with a spiral 
form: with the formation of higher levels and the “leaving” or alternation 
of lower levels, which in their turn serve the possibility of the further develop- 
ment of the system as a whole. Thus interlevel investigations, being inter- 
disciplinary, also exclude understanding them as reducing one level to another 
or attempting to find their correlative connections and coordinations. I es- 
pecially emphasize this because if in his time N. N. Lange spoke about psy- 
chophysiological parallelism as about a “terrible” thought, then at this time 
reductionism has become a truly terrible thought for psychology. A recogni- 
tion of this is penetrating ever more into western science. The general con- 
clusion from an analysis of reductionism was most sharply formulated by 
English authors in the latest (1974) issue of the international journal Cogni- 
tion. The only alternative to reductionism is dialectic materialism (S. Rose 
and H. Rose, Vol. 2, No. 4). This is actually so. Scientific resolution of the 
problem, biological and psychological, psychological and social, is simply 
impossible outside the Marxist system of analysis. For this reason even the 
positivist program “United Science” (with capital letters!), pretending to 
unite knowledge by means of universal cybernetics and multimathematical 
(model) schemes, suffered a clear failure. 
Although these schemes are actually capable of comparing the different 
phenomena qualitatively among themselves, yet they are not effective at the 
given level of abstraction, at the level of specifics of these phenomena and 
their intertransformations. As far as psychology is concerned, there it defini- 
tively breaks with the concreteness of man. 
Of course, having said all this, I had in mind most of all the relations be- 
tween psychological and morphophysiological levels of investigation. One 
CONCLUSION 
143 
must think, however, that the matter also is the same in the connection that 
exists between the social and psychological levels. 
Unfortunately, specifically those social—psychological problems re- 
main the least researched in our science that are the most overgrown with con- 
ceptions and methods drawn from foreign research, that is, from research sub- 
ordinated to the problem of finding a psychological basis for justifying and im- 
mortalizing interhuman relations engendered by bourgeois society. But a re- 
construction of social—psy chological science from the Marxist point of view can- 
not take place independently from one or another social—psychological un- 
derstanding of man, and the role in his formulation of vital connections of 
man with the world engendered by these social relations in which he acts. 
For this reason, thinking about the perspectives of psychological science 
as centering in itself multifaceted approaches to man, one must not be dis- 
tracted from that fact that this centering takes place on the social level — just 
as it is at this level that human fate is decided. 
Supplement: Psychological 
Questions on the Consciousness of 
Learning 
In not one psychological problem does intellectualism so sharply disclose 
its indefensibility as in the psychological problem of consciousness. Intel- 
lectualistic treatment of consciousness turns it into a simple synonym for 
thought and understanding. But are consciousness and thought in reality 
one and the same thing? 
Consciousness and thought do not directly or simply coincide with each 
other. Consciousness cannot be deduced from thought for consciousness 
is not determined by thought, it is determined by being — the real life of 
man. 
The concept consciousness is not simply a wider concept of thought. 
Consciousness is not thought plus perception plus memory plus ability, or 
even all of these processes taken together plus emotional experience. Con- 
sciousness must be psychologically disclosed in its true characteristics. It 
must be understood not only as knowing but also as relations, as direction. 
It must be noted that contrary to the intellectualistic pedagogy of Meiman, 
Lay, and others, Russian pedagogical thought was preparing a full-fledged 
understanding of consciousness. It was prepared by those who considered 
education and nurture as processes not only of giving a child knowledge but 
also as giving direction to his personality, his relation to reality. 
“Real education,” wrote Dobrolyubov in his time, “‘is education which 
compels a determination of its relation to everything that surrounds it.” And 
145 
146 
SUPPLEMENT 
this is the main thing that characterizes consciousness, that makes man “moral 
not because of habit, but because of consciousness.” The same thought in es- 
sence was expressed also by Chernyshevskii, who required first of all educa- 
tion of “‘man in the real sense of the word.” 
In a brilliant although very unique form the problem of the education of 
the personality was posed by Ushinskii. “Let us suppose that a child learns 
some verses in a foreign language which he does not understand; consequently 
he learned only the sounds in their sequence, one after the other. Conscious- 
ness takes part in this learning; without participation of attention the child 
would not hear the sounds; without participation of judgment he would not 
recognize the difference or similarity between these sounds... 
. 
“But let us suppose finally that the child became a youth, and that in 
the soul of the youth there arose a question to which the thought contained 
in the poetry is the answer, or there arose a feeling for which the poetry is 
a more complete poetic expression — then the kernel which is contained in 
the poetry is released from all of its wrappings, passes into the spiritual 
memory of the youth, and does this not in the form of verses, not in the 
form of words, even not in the form of thought, but in the form of anew 
spiritual force so that the youth does not at all think about these verses, 
does not even remember the thought contained in them, but after learning 
them will look at everything with a somewhat changed look, will feel some- 
what different, will want not exactly that which he wanted formerly — that 
is, in other words, man will develop to a higher degree.””! 
It is not enough to learn the words, not enough to understand the words, 
not enough to understand even the thoughts and the feelings contained in 
them; it is necessary that these thoughts and these feelings should begin to 
determine personality internally. This simple idea expresses the most im- 
portant conclusion prompted by life experiences of educating man. For that 
reason it is close to and understood by all of those who, like Dobrolyubov, 
Ushinskii, and Tolstoy, approached school, education, and upbringing in 
general, first of all from the aspect of the demands on man (what kind of 
aman is necessary to us, what kind must he be?) and not only from the 
aspect of requirements directed toward knowledge, thoughts, feelings (what 
kind of knowledge, ideas, feelings, what kind must they be?). 
The difference between these two approaches is in no way a play on 
words; its decisive significance must be understood completely. 
We have become so used to thinking of consciousness in concepts hypo- 
thesizing psychic life of man in the form of separate psychological functions 
or capabilities (psychic functions are nothing else than disguised capabili- 
ties) that the difference between these two approaches is mostly effaced, 
especially from the psychological point of view. The illusion consists here of 
1K. D. Ushinskii, Collected Works, Vol. 8, Moscow—Leningrad, 1950, pp. 363-364. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
147 
the fact that what man thinks and how he feels and for what he strives 
seems to depend on what kind of thoughts, feelings, desires he has. But, 
after all, thinking does not think, feelings do not feel, and desires do not 
desire — man thinks, feels, and desires. This means that the main thing is, 
what do the thoughts and knowledge that we impart to man, those feelings 
we evoke in him, those desires that awake in him mean for man himself? 
Knowledge and thoughts that are assimilated through thought may not, how- 
ever, become the achievement of man himself and then they will be dead; the 
education of feelings may engender a simple sentimentality (a clear example of 
this in James: A baroness sheds tears in the theater over the suffering of one 
of the simple people while her serf coachman freezes in the fierce cold at the 
theater entrance). The very best intentions that we inspire in the young may 
become the kind of intentions about which it was said: The road to hell is 
paved with good intentions. 
For this reason the vital and true approach to education is that approach 
to the individual problems of rearing and education that stems from the 
requirements confronting man — what kind of a man must he be in life and 
with what must he be equipped, what must his knowledge be, his feelings, 
and his thought? 
Let us take the question of Marxist education as it is posed by such an 
eminent vital pedagogue as M. I Kalinin. What does he require from the 
teachers of Marxism—Leninism? He requires that they teach in such a way 
that Marxism—Leninism would enter into the life of man as an organic need 
for him to live in one way and not another. “If I were a teacher of Marxism— 
Leninism, then I would always turn my attention especially to how my 
students essentially assimilate the basis of Marxism—Leninism. After all, it is 
possible to assimilate Marxism—Leninism only formally, it is possible to learn 
all the formulas, historical events, and dates very well but not to become a 
convinced Marxist—Leninist in spite of this. What do you think, is this pos- 
sible or not? (Voices from the audience: “It’s possible!’’) On the other hand, 
it is possible not to know the formulas, but in essence to have the spirit of 
Marxism—Leninism.? 
But you may retort, what kind of relation does all of this actually have 
to the principle of consciousness? 
All of this, of course, refers directly to the principle of the consciousness 
of learning that is disclosed in its true content and from the right aspect — 
from the aspect of man: ‘Man is man. It is necessary to begin from this,” * 
And so two different approaches to consciousness exist. Correspondingly, 
two different concepts of consciousness also exist. One reduces the require- 
ments emanating from the principle of consciousness to requirements in rela- 
tion to thought, understanding of cognitive processes in general, plus perhaps 
2M. I, Kalinin, Questions of Communistic Education, Moscow, 1940, p. 10. 
3 Ibid., p. 43. 
148 
SUPPLEMENT 
also abilities, emotions, and habits. This understanding of consciousness is 
based on an abstract psychology of function.* 
The other understanding is less “psychological” but more vital and honest 
and is derived from the requirements of man and his personality. It does not 
dissipate the problem of consciousness in the totality of questions about 
separate psychic processes; from the point of view of this understanding con- 
sciousness is that in the personality of man which characterizes his knowledge 
as well as his thought, his feelings, and his desires, that which they really 
become for man and toward which they direct his life. 
In order to explain the problem of consciousness scientifically, if it is 
understood in this way, it is necessary to start from the concrete psycho- 
logical teaching about consciousness. And for this it is necessary first of all 
to admit that the problem of consciousness in psychology is a separate prob- 
lem that cannot be reduced either to general philosophical positions about 
consciousness or to the sum of the problems of particular psychic processes. 
This should be considered especially because there is an abysmal lack of 
understanding of it. 
Completely developed psychological teaching about consciousness is a 
matter for the future. But even now we have certain initial concrete psycho- 
logical representations about consciousness compiled on the basis of research 
work that we have been carrying out in this area for a number of years. 
In a paper it is impossible, of course, to give a report of everything that 
has been done. We will enlarge briefly only on certain psychological positions 
about consciousness. 
In analyzing the process of cognition by a child of material to be learned, 
there naturally arise two questions: What does the child recognize® in the 
material and how does he recognize it? Both of these questions usually seem 
somewhat simple and do not present any problem in themselves, and for this 
reason they escape investigation. 
The first question appears to be particularly without content. Actually, 
the child whose attention is drawn to one type of material or another to be 
learned obviously is conscious precisely of the given material. Actually, how- 
ever, this question is much more complicated, and if it is not to be formally 
approached, it deserves the most intent examination. In order to demonstrate 
this we will take the simplest examples from the practice of teaching spelling. 
*In essence it is, of course, an idealistic concept: It is that man’s consciousness is determined by his 
psychic processes formed in education, that is, in cognitive, theoretical relations to reality, that my 
consciousness is determined by consciousness itself — consciousness of others. 
The word recognize in this section is used in the sense of “to perceive consciously and deliberately, 
to attend to.” — Translator’s note. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
149 
The child is given an exercise: to read a riddle, to guess it, and then to 
draw what he guessed and write the text of the riddle under the drawing. This 
kind of exercise, proposed by D. N. Ushakov, is meant to ensure that it is 
done consciously, and actually it cannot be done mechanically without rec- 
ognizing in it the material suggested. In the first place the child unavoidably 
must be conscious of the riddle — the meaning of the words in which it is 
expressed, the thought contained in them — otherwise he will not be able to 
guess it. Further, he must inevitably recognize the answer to the same extent 
in order to portray it in the form of a drawing. Thus, finally, the child must 
write the text of the riddle, the content of which is fully known to him in 
advance. 
Let us move, however, to a consideration of the example from another 
aspect. We will ask ourselves, of what does the task of the given exercise 
consist, why was it assigned? Of course, the first task was not to teach the 
child to guess riddles, just as it was not to teach him to draw; it was given 
to teach the child spelling. But consciousness of just the orthographic aspect 
of the text in the given exercise is decidedly not ensured. After all, the only 
word about which there may have been some doubt about spelling in the 
consciousness of the child was the word riddle, but the child did not need to 
write this word, only to illustrate it with a picture. In this exercise con- 
ciousness of the text that is read actually is always there, but the child is not 
conscious of what is required for consciously mastering spelling. 
One must not think that the example given constitutes an exception. 
The same thing happens when a child is given the task of making a list of 
names of cows and a separate list of names of dogs. For this assignment a 
child must recognize which names would more likely be cows’ names and 
which dogs’, and he actually honestly tries to penetrate the question of may 
there exist, for example, a dog with the name “‘Zor’ka,” or does this name 
belong exclusively to cows? But having done this exercise, unfortunately 
he must learn something else: that personal names of animals (and this is 
completely the same whether it is names of dogs or names of cows) are all 
written with capital letters. The same thing exactly takes place in certain 
exercises in supplying words that have been left out of a text and in certain 
arithmetic problems and exercises. 
This lack of correspondence between the supposed and the actual 
content recognized by the child in the process of his learning activity places 
us before a psychological question about the subject of consciousness that 
is more general in its significance — the question of what exactly is recognized 
by man and under which conditions. 
The usual answer to this question is that the subject of consciousness is 
that to which attention is directed. Reference to attention here, however, 
does not explain anything and cannot explain anything inasmuch as it leads 
only to a muddling of the question, making its solution completely hopeless, 
150 
SUPPLEMENT 
or at best simply producing it in another form — as a question of the subject 
of attention. 
Let us turn to experimental facts. 
In one of the investigations of P. I. Zinchenko® these experiments were 
set up: The subject was given a large sheet that had fifteen objects drawn on 
it and the same number of separate picture cards, which could be placed on 
this sheet as is done in ordinary Bingo with pictures, but in this case the 
pictures on the sheet and the pictures on the cards were different. 
One set of subjects was required to distribute the cards so that the first 
letter of the name of the object pictured on the card corresponded with the 
first letter of the name of the object pictured on the sheet; others were 
required to distribute the cards according to some kind of relation between 
pictures on the cards and the drawings, for example, saw — axe, book — 
glasses, etc. 
It is understood that in both the first and second instances, the objects 
drawn on the cards fell into the subject’s “field of attention” similarly; 
without paying any attention to what was drawn on the cards it was im- 
possible, of course, for the subject to carry out either the first or the second 
of these tasks. What, however, was the object of consciousness of the subjects 
in the one case and in the other? 
In order to answer this question we will follow the further course of the 
experiment. After the pictures were removed from the table the subject was 
unexpectedly confronted with the problem of remembering what was 
pictured on the cards. 
A comparison of data obtained in both variants of the experiment gave 
the following results: The subjects who selected the cards according to 
initial letters of the names of the items pictured were able to name these 
objects in a relatively insignificant number in comparison with the subjects 
who selected pictures according to the relation of the items pictured. This 
difference was even greater in a paired reproduction. Certain subjects having 
gone through the first variant of the experiment were not able to remember 
even one pair of drawings, whereas the subjects having gone through the 
second variant of the experiment requiring the selection of pictures according 
to relations of items pictured yielded very high indices. 
Thus remembering items pictured on cards, which of course caught the 
subjects’ attention in the same way in the process of carrying out both tasks, 
appeared sharply different, however. This fact is explained by the following. 
In the experiment cited, the subject selecting the cards according to 
initial letter of the word representing the object drawn sees, distinguishes, 
and selects a given object (let us say he has it in his field of attention) but 
actually the object of his consciousness is the letter composition of the word: 
°Partially published in a paper, “Problems of involuntary memory,” Scientific Reports of the Kharkov 
Institute of Foreign Languages, Vol. 1, 1939. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
151 
the name of this item. In carrying out the second task, the object of con- 
sciousness of the subject is the pictured item itself and its relation to 
another object drawn on the chart. 
For this reason it can so easily, 
and perhaps at will, be reestablished in the consciousness of the subject, partic- 
ularly easily in the paired method of remembering. After all, that which has not 
been perceived generally is not reproducible at will — thisis a completely indis- 
putable rule (although, it is understood, it does not have any reverse strength). 
This rule, incidentally, is used almost always to establish whether one phenom- 
enon or another was perceived, requiring in those cases an accounting, that is, 
requiring specially a reproduction at will of the object in the consciousness. 
What lies behind this difference in perception? Evidently the different 
content of the subject’s activity in carrying out both tasks, which are cam- 
parable. In other words, the subject’s actual object of consciousness depends 
on what kind of activity he was carrying out. Thus in the first question we 
were trying to approach, we were confronted by a highly general problem 
of disclosing the internal connections of activity and consciousness. 
We have seen that the presence of one or another content in the field of 
perception (or if convenient, in the field of attention) of the child does not 
yet mean that this content is the subject of his consciousness, that it is 
recognized by him. Perception is not always connected with consciousness, 
that is, with that special process that French authors translate with the term 
prise de conscience. 
At first glance this assertion seems somewhat paradoxical; nevertheless, 
this is so. That which is perceived and the conscious content do not coincide 
directly. 
Am I conscious, for example, of unevenness of a sidewalk along which I 
am walking, of people coming toward me, objects, the displays in shop 
windows at which I cast a glance at the same time I am engrossed in a con- 
versation with my companion? No. The object of my consciousness in this 
case is only the content of what my friend is saying to me. Does this mean, 
however, that I do not perceive the surroundings? My movements, my whole 
behavior in the street are in exact correlation with what is going on around 
me, and consequently I do perceive it. 
Subjectively, according to direct self-observation, that which is perceived 
and that which I am conscious of are, however, indistinguishable. The fact 
is that as soon as I ask myself whether I am conscious of a given phenomenon, 
in this act itself it becomes the subject of my consciousness, it is recognized. 
This psychological fact lies at the basis of the illusion about the direct cor- 
respondence between what is perceived and what is recognized. Actually, the 
circle of what is recognized is relatively small. This has been known for a 
long time. 
152 
SUPPLEMENT 
In modern man consciousness is “the universal form” of psychic reflec- 
tion of the world, but from this it only follows that everything that is per- 
ceived may become, under given conditions, the subject of consciousness, 
and not at all that all psychic reflection has the form of consciousness. 
Which of the numberless and many-faceted phenomena perceived by 
man is he conscious of? 
If we proceed from the fact that recognizing material to be learned is a 
necessary condition of learning — and this, it is understood, is indisputably 
so and we will yet see the whole importance of this — then we must know 
how to answer this question with absolute precision. 
In traditional psychology the resolution of the question as to exactly 
what enters the subject’s “‘field of clear consciousness” was reduced to in- 
dicating the role of separate factors — external and internal. External factors 
are those properties of the objects themselves — as, for example, intensity 
of their actions on the sense organs, their novelty or uncommonness, even 
the space they occupy, etc. /nternal factors are, for example, interest in the 
given object, its emotional colorings, the presence of volitional force, active 
apperception, etc. In general, understanding the superficial facts accurately, 
such a resolution of the question does not disclose any kind of 
internal regular 
relationship, and for this reason it is essentially an invalid resolution. 
An analysis based on research data concerning the development of forms 
of psychic reflection and internal dependence of them on the structure of 
the subject’s activity leads to an entirely different resolution of the problem 
of the subject of consciousness. These investigations allowed the establish- 
ment of the two following positions, most important for the question under 
consideration. 
The first of these is that, as we have already said, activity affecting the 
subject may be reflected by him in its properties, connections, or relations, 
and this reflection may mediate the activity of the subject; nonetheless, the 
subject may not be conscious of this activity. Using a term of analytical 
psychology, it may be said that the subjective image of reality engendering 
and directing the activity of the subject may, in spite of this, not be “presented” 
to him. This area of the “not presented” in the psyche of man is extraordi- 
narily broad, which incidentally may make all psychology limiting the sub- 
ject of its study only to phenomena accessible to introspection completely 
devitalized and false. 
The second position is that the content “presenting itself” (according to 
the terminology of Stout) to the subject that is actually recognized by him 
is the content that occupies a completely determined structural place in his 
activity and is the subject of his action (the direct goal of a given action) — 
external or internal. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
153 
Let us explain this position. Activity has a determined internal structure. 
One of the processes entering into the structure of man’s activity is action. 
Action is that goal-directed process not engendered by the goal alone but 
by the motive of the activity as a whole, which the given action realizes. 
For example, I go to the library. This is an action; like every action it is 
directed to a determined, concrete, direct goal (‘‘to arrive at the library”’). 
And this goal in itself engenders my action. I go to the library because I am 
looking for something I need in the study of literature. This motive causes 
me to set myself the given goal and to carry out the corresponding action. 
Under other circumstances the same motive might have evoked entirely dif- 
ferent actions; for instance, I might have gone not to the library but to my 
friend who has the literature I need. How is the direct goal of the action rec- 
ognized? In order that I might recognize it, I must of course realize its rela- 
tion to the motive of the activity. In order to study literature, it is necessary 
to go to the library. Thus that which occupies in activity the structural place 
of the goal of some kind of personal action must necessarily appear to the 
subject (be reflected by him) in its relation to the motive of his activity, and 
this means that it must be recognized. 
Thus the question of whether a given content does or does not enter into 
the ‘‘field of consciousness” is resolved independently of what that content 
is in itself. It makes no difference whether or not it is presented, for example, 
in the form of intensely affecting stimuli, whether it is, for example, marked 
by novelty or is habitual, etc. It does not even depend on interests, inclina- 
tions, or emotions of the perceiving subject but is determined by the place 
of this content in the structure of his activity: Only that content is actually 
recognized which appears before the subject as an object to which one action 
or another of his is directly aimed. In other words, in order that the perceived 
content be recognized, it is necessary that it occupy the structural place of a 
direct goal of action in the subject’s activity, and thus that it appear in a cor- 
responding relation to the motive of this activity. This position is valid with 
respect to internal and external, practical and theoretical activity. 
The pupil writes. What is he conscious of? First of all, this depends on 
what makes him write. But let us put this question aside for the time being 
and assume that from one motive or another a goal appeared before him: to 
inform, to express his thought in writing. Then the subject of consciousness 
will be just this thought, its expression in words. Of course, here the pupil will 
perceive the formation of the letters he writes, but this will not at the given 
moment (that is, actually) be the subject of his consciousness, and the letter, 
the word, or the sentence for him subjectively will only appear as written in 
one way or another, well or poorly. Let us now suppose that in the same 
activity his goal has become something else: to write beautifully, calligraph- 
154 
SUPPLEMENT 
ically. Then the actual subject of his consciousness will become precisely 
the formation of the letters.’ 
Here it is understood that it is not the structural place occupied by the 
given content in activity that depends on whether or not this content is rec- 
ognized but, conversely, the fact of recognizing the given content depends 
on its structural place in activity. 
The truth of this is demonstrated particularly by the well-known psy- 
chological fact that the only way to keep any kind of content as the object 
of one’s consciousness is to act in relation to this content, otherwise it 
quickly stops being recognized and leaves the “field of consciousness.” This 
fact is stressed very clearly in the well-known book of K. S. Stanislavskii when 
he analyzes what it means to keep one’s attention on some kind of an ob- 
ject and how this is done.® 
These transformations of perceived but not recognized content into ac- 
tually recognized, and the converse, depend on a change in the place oc- 
cupied by the given content in the structure of activity, and may at present 
be understood neurophysiologically also. 
Current research indicates that physiologically all activity represents a 
system of processes (‘‘a functional system,” according to the terminology of 
P. K. Anokhin), directed by signals uninterruptedly emanating from the ex- 
ternal environment as well as from the organism itself (for example, vessel— 
muscle sensations). These signal-stimuli unite, are integrated by various 
sensory nervous centers, distributed in the cortex as well as in the subcortical 
regions, and connected with various motor centers. Depending on the “‘level” 
of the central nervous system at which the union of sensory signals and their 
transmission to motor nervous paths takes place, various neurological “‘levels 
of structure” of processes are distinguished (N. A. Bernshtein). In directing 
complex processes several “levels” participate simultaneously. These levels, 
however, do not have equal rights. Some of them are dominant, while others 
play the role of a background (“background level” in the terminology of 
Bernshtein). Here it is remarkable in the first place that activity expressed 
externally with similar movements may be structured at various neurological 
levels depending on what kind of stimuli play the main role in it. Secondly, 
it is remarkable (as N. A. Bernshtein especially emphasizes) that stimuli of 
the dominant level are always recognized no matter which level this is.? Thus 
Let us note incidentally that the secret fact of so-called complication is nothing else than an expres- 
sion under experimental laboratory conditions of the same law of consciousness about which we are 
speaking. The goal “of determining the moment of the ringing of the bell” makes the sound of the 
bell pouty recognized, the arrow of the apparatus only “indicates” a certain division of the scale, 
that is, “it lags” or vice versa. Compare also the data obtained in experimental investigation of ab- 
straction by O. Kyul’tse. 
®See K. S. Stanislavskii, An Actor’s Work with Himself, Moscow, 1958, Chapter 5. 
°N. A. Bernshtein, “On the question of the nature and dynamics of coordination of functions,” 
Scholarly Papers, Moscow State University, Vol. 90, 1945. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
155 
the recognized content affecting activity afferently differs with varying 
neurological structures. The structure itself is determined by what N. A. 
Bernshtein calls the task, which is precisely what we call goal in our terminol- 
ogy. We call something that is somewhat different a task: It is a goal given 
under specific conditions, the content of a process that depends especially 
on the conditions of attaining the required result, physiologically realized 
not by the dominant level but, conversely, by the levels that bring it about. 
Thus when we are dealing with any activity — for example, learning — 
very little of what is perceived by the subject, and without which the carry- 
ing out of activity is impossible, is also actually recognized by him. In spite 
of what is apparent, what is actually recognized is only that which enters into 
the activity as a subject of one or another action that is carried out as its 
direct goal. 
This circumstance naturally presents us with a problem — to investigate the 
process of the transformation itself of the given content into the actually 
recognized, that is, the process as aresult of which the given content occupies 
its proper place in activity. 
As I have already said, the process described in psychology under the 
name, process of attention, does not, strictly speaking, correspond with the 
process of consciousness, with the process prise de conscience. In the usual 
use of the words, however, the question of what the subject recognizes has 
usually been expressed in the form of a question about what his attention is 
directed to. In order not to depart from this terminology, we will maintain 
this convention in these pages. Then our problem will appear in the form of 
the traditional problem of attracting and maintaining the attention of the 
pupil on the given subject. 
In working with younger school children this problem is especially acute. 
At first glance the main difficulty with which the teacher is confronted is that 
the attention of the child is constantly distracted, shows its lack of stability, 
its incapability of being maintained fora long time on one content or another 
— on the explanations of the teacher, on the textbooks being used in class, 
on the written text, etc. 
What is the nature of this phenomenon? Does the given phenomenon ex- 
press a special property of the attention of a child, some kind of an inability 
characteristic of him? 
The fact that this is not so is decisively borne out by the fact that in other 
circumstances the child displays a very great stability of attention, a very 
long concentration on the subject. Under experimental conditions in initially 
solving the problem of structural analysis of activity in the laboratory of 
156 
SUPPLEMENT 
genetic psychology of the Ukrainian Psychoneurological Academy we were 
able to establish in children who were seven or seven and a half years old an 
uninterrupted concentration lasting 20 to 30, and in some cases, 40 minutes 
with a very small number of distractions. Thus in one of the investigations 
(V. A. Asnin and T. O. Ginevskaya, 1935) the following average indices were 
obtained: average length of concentration — 22.3 minutes, average number 
of distractions in the experiment — 2.5; and in another, still greater indices: 
average length of concentration — 26 minutes, average number of distractions 
—a total of 0.8, that is, fewer than one case on the average for each experi- 
ment (P. I. Zinchenko, 1935). 
On the other hand, the interpretation given above of the distractibility 
of attention is contradicted by the fact that this phenomenon quite fre- 
quently very clearly seems to be only the negative side of a contrasting 
phenomenon, an increase in the child’s concentration on another (extraneous) 
object. 
In research already cited, the dependence of stability of activity on the 
real structure of the given activity is completely obvious. Actually a simple 
“turning” of attention of the child to something by means of suitable direc- 
tion, for example, can evoke in him only an initial orienting reaction, which 
is later naturally extinguished if no activity develops in connection with it. 
If, however, the given object maintains itself in the attention field of the 
child, then this depends on the child’s being confronted by a recognized 
problem and his acting in relation to the object that now occupies the 
structural place of a goal in his activity. Thus the question here is not a ques- 
tion of the special features of the child’s attention as some kind of ability of 
his consciousness but of the characteristics of his activity. 
When a student hears some kind of explanation he is active internally, 
although externally he may preserve a complete immobility. If he is internal- 
ly passive, inactive, then he will not understand anything and nothing will 
enter his consciousness. But it is not enough, of course, to be active in general. 
It is necessary that the activity should relate to that which is being set forth 
or shown. Actually, we practically never meet with a condition of “inactivity” 
of the student. Inactivity is prostration, sleep; man’s behavior, emanating 
constantly from activity, is the behavior, for example of Tolstoy’s Joe, the 
child servant in “Notes from the Pickwick Club,” and not at all the behavior 
of an attentive man. 
In cases when attention was distracted, the resulting internal activity 
(usually the activity of perceiving some kind of material) was rapidly dis- 
turbed and changed to another activity, in youngsters — frequently external, 
in older children — internal. The child continues to sit without moving, 
fixing his eyes on the teacher or the blackboard, but he is already not at the 
lesson, he dropped out and is thinking of something else. We agree with K. S. 
Stanislavskii who says that attention does not consist of “staring” at the 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
157 
object but of being active in relation to the given object. This means that to 
make the pupil attentive, to engage his attention, it is necessary first of all 
to organize the activity required of him and to encourage its determined 
aspects and forms. It is this way and only this way that the question stands. 
One of the important circumstances that leads to the instability of learn- 
ing activity in younger children is that to a significant degree this activity oc- 
curs in the form of internal theoretical activity, in the form of the act of 
perception, that is, action answering a cognitive goal. It is necessary not to 
follow some practical action by looking or listening but specifically to learn 
or understand something. Perception excluded here from practical activity is 
in itself the action realizing the learning activity of the child. On the other 
hand, as a rule, it is deprived of a motive lying in the very content of what is 
perceived; it is specifically an action and not an activity and thus quite 
abstract: Its motive not only does not coincide with its direct goal but is 
complexly related to it. 
How difficult such an action is for children of preschool age is demon- 
strated particularly by the investigation by N. G. Morozova of preschoolers’ 
understanding or, more precisely, recognizing verbal explanations. This re- 
search indicated that outside direct motivation and explanation of “‘instruc- 
tions” given earlier, that is, before the intended action, even if it is com- 
pelling, is heard by the children, but it hardly “‘reaches” them. This does not 
happen because they do not understand it; the same explanation is fully 
accepted by them when it is directly included in carrying out external ac- 
tivity or when the specific motivation for the corresponding cognitive goal 
is consciously recognized by the children. 
School learning is remarkable just for the reason that in its very essence 
it unavoidably requires certain internal capabilities of the child who is learn- 
ing theoretically to act under conditions of learning activity, which is usually 
cognitive. This is a new requirement for the child. Internal theoretical pro- 
cesses in the system of complexly motivated cognitive relations in children 
who are just beginning to study are something they still must master or have 
just begun to master. For this reason these processes are least stable in them. 
This is expressed in the difficulty young school children have in maintaining 
attention for a long time on the object of internal theoretical action. 
How, in general, is it possible to develop internal action in others? We 
can scarcely write an adequately clear description of such action, and only 
requiring it, or only organizing the problem is too little to achieve it. At the 
beginning the main procedure is to act together: for example, the teacher 
points out something in the object, describes it in a given sequence and in 
a given system, specifically pointing out in it one thing and skipping another 
that is nonessential or incidental; or he gives an oral evaluation, an analysis 
of it and reaches conclusions, etc. Here it is assumed that the pupil is paying 
attention to him and following in thought the external expressions of his 
158 
SUPPLEMENT 
theoretical actions and in this way following the course of thought “for 
himself.’”’ But this is exactly what cannot be achieved sometimes. Naturally, 
for this reason there arises the necessity to develop certain methods that 
would make it easier for the teacher to lead the pupils after him. Among 
these empirically discovered methods an important place is occupied by 
visual methods using the object itself, and where it is possible, external ac- 
tions of the pupils themselves. The latter plays a particularly large role when 
the pupil must independently achieve the action demonstrated for him, for 
example, in the process of preparing lessons. It is well known, for example, 
how important it is to give pupils in lower classes completely precise instruc- 
tions about the order of carrying out the learning task, to require from them 
a specific external arrangement of material in written work, etc. Actually 
even the writing down of the questions in solving problems plays specifically 
the same role of “‘bringing into the open” the theoretical action carried out 
by the learner. In general, the problems of structuring and directing theoretical 
actions through which the child learns, specifically the problem of directing 
his perception and consequently the direction of his attention, has great 
pedagogical significance. We cannot, however, consider this now. From the 
point of view of the problem of consciousness it is sufficient for us to consider 
only two specific problems that arise here: first, the question of the visual 
aspect of the perceived object and, second, the question of the role of con- 
scious, goal-directed learning activity of the child’s operations and habits. 
We will turn our attention to the first of these questions first. 
Our approach to the problem of visual methods is often extremely over- 
simplified — I might say, naively sensualistic. This is justifiably pointed out 
by N. K. Goncharov in his paper about the philosophical bases of pedagogy. !° 
In one of the unpublished investigations of the Kharkov Pedagogical Institute 
(V. V. Mistyuk, 1936) this problem was submitted to experimental investiga- 
tion from the point of view of analysis of the actual role of visual material in 
various processes of schooling and at various stages of the development of 
these processes. Incidentally, this problem was also elucidated in a series of 
other psychological investigations (L. I. Bozhovich, A. V. Zaporozhets, and 
others), particularly in a group of experimental works on children’s percep- 
tion of drawing-illustrations. ™! 
Summarizing the data of this research and the research of other authors, 
it is necessary first to note the obvious fact that the function of visual 
1° Soviet Pedagogy, No. 7, 1944. 
“Partially published in Scientific Reports of the Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, Vol. 1, 1939; Vol. 
6, 1941 (papers of K. E. Khomenko, T. D. Ginevskaya, and V. V. Mistyuk). 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
159 
material may be quite varied. It is one thing when in the process of teaching 
there arises the problem of giving the pupils a lively, colorful picture of a 
piece of activity that is not sufficiently well known to them, or broadening 
their sensory experience in this way, or enriching their impressions, in a word 
making one group of phenomena or another more concrete, more real, and 
more precisely represented for them. A visit to a zoo, a historical picture, a 
photograph of a writer or scientist, etc., all of these make that about which 
the child is learning more concrete and as if more tangible for him; the animal 
world appears in all of its multifaceted nature, historical experience is more 
sharply felt and acquires the feeling of the epoch, the appearance of an ac- 
tivist known to the child becomes closer and better known. All of this is 
very necessary and very important. 
It is something else when visual material is incorporated directly into 
the process of teaching in connection with a special pedagogical problem. 
I have in mind such situations as the use of visual material for the initial 
teaching of arithmetic, lessons in Russian, in-class exercises in physics, etc. 
Here the role of visual material does not, of course, consist, for example, 
of having the pictures of pencils on the board enrich the sensory experience 
of the child; they serve not to do this but to teach counting. Similarly, a 
demonstration at a lesson of the phenomenon of floating bodies in a liquid 
or the phenomenon of distribution of heat is not done to show the child that 
these phenomena do occur in nature; the main task here is to show the child 
the essence of these phenomena, to explain their laws, to lead the child to 
the appropriate scientific generalizations. 
Various forms of visual materials may and must be distinguished further, 
but for our purposes it is important to single out only the second type of 
visual materials mentioned since it is specifically in relation to this type of 
visual materials that the question about the object of consciousness arises 
with particular sharpness. In what does the psychological function of visual 
material of this type consist? Evidently in its serving as an external support 
for internal actions carried out by the child under the direction of the teacher 
in the process of mastering knowledge. This type of visual material in itself 
is not a direct object of the learning actions of the child. It only represents 
this object: After all the child is not learning how to count notebooks but 
counting; he’s studying not floating or sinking bodies but the rules of float- 
ing and the law of Archimedes; not the heating of bodies but the laws of con- 
vection, etc. And least of all does the matter here pertain to the problem of 
concretization of representation of what the learner already knows; rather 
the reverse is true, it consists in generalizing what he knows. This means that 
visual material represents in these cases precisely the material in which and 
through which the material to be learned actually still must be found. 
The question of the object of consciousness appears with respect to the
