problem under consideration in another form also: not only as a question of 
oD) 
160 
SUPPLEMENT 
what the learner is factually conscious of but also as a question of what he 
must become conscious of in connection with the given pedagogical problem. 
From the psychological point of view, this is the central question in the prob- 
lem of visual material. 
In order to demonstrate this we will turn to an analysis of certain simple 
examples of the use of visual materials. 
At one time I was shown several class charts, enthusiastically executed 
by a teacher, showing groups of similar objects; These charts were to serve 
the purpose of teaching first graders numbers and operations with quantities 
within the limits of ten. These charts were distinguished from others not in 
the principle of their construction but by the precision of their execution 
and the character of the objects portrayed on them. Thus, for example, on 
one of them were drawn tanks and antiaircraft guns. The intention of the 
author was obvious: He wanted to produce an interesting visual aid that 
would easily attract the attention of the first graders and would be maximal- 
ly concrete and lifelike (this occurred during the Second World War). 
Now let ussee this visual aid in action. In the first place, quite undoubted- 
ly such a visual aid would actually attract the attention of pupils and keep it. 
This means that the very first and simplest task is solved — the attention of 
the pupils is attracted, but it is attracted to the chart. Is it attracted, how- 
ever, to the subject of study — quantities and quantitative relationships? The 
psychologically incorrect assumption here is that if an object appears in the 
“field of consciousness” of the pupil, then in this itself the pupil is also con- 
scious of everything that is objectively contained in the given object. In the 
example under consideration, the incorrectness of this assumption is evident. 
We saw that the “process of attention,” the process of recognition, is not a 
formal, but a cogent and goal-directed process, that it is connected to an 
internal action. For this reason the first and most important question with 
which we must approach the analysis of the visual aid under consideration is 
the question of what kind of action must it produce and to what kind of 
goal must this action be directed. Let us assume that this action must consist 
of abstracting the quantitative significance and adding numbers. Is this pre- 
cisely the action that is evoked in the child by the portrayal of a certain num- 
ber of tanks and guns, etc.? Not at all, of course. The tanks pictured strongly 
attract the child’s attention because they are cogent for him in their own 
right: What kind of tanks are they, why are they in a column, why are they 
directed aganst each other when they are all marked with the Russian star, 
why are the stars small and not large, etc. — this is what makes up the in- 
ternal mental activity that in itself keeps the attention of the child on the 
chart for a relatively long time and determines what he will be conscious Or, 
that is, what the object of his consciousness will be. 
But the teacher directs the pupils. He puts before them the learning goals 
and tries by this means to organize their activity in another direction; in the 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
161 
given instance, however, this direction decidedly does not correspond with 
how the activity of the pupils develops under the influence of the visual 
material itself. The internal actions that must be structured in the learner 
require an abstraction from the subject content of the picture, and the richer 
the content, the more different this is. Counting uninteresting pencils is 
psychologically easier for the child than counting interesting tanks. When 
the child is drawn away from the formal quantitative significance by other 
content significance of the same objects, then directing his activity is more 
difficult than when he is distracted by something extraneous. When, for ex- 
ample, he simply looks out the window, it is possible to require that he look 
at the blackboard; in the first case his whole attention is concentrated on 
the visual aid, but his consciousness is not on the numbers and not on their 
relations but on the military pictures. Externally he is oriented to the same 
thing as the teacher; internally, however, he is following not the teacher but 
the subject content pictured on the chart. 
Of course, in the analysis of this example the whole question is greatly 
simplified and schematized. But the main thing is presented quite correctly 
nevertheless. It is that inasmuch as consciousness is the result of the content 
directed to a specified goal of the process, then the introduction into the 
study of visual materials must certainly consider at least the two following 
psychological points: (1) What kind of a concrete role must the visual 
material play in learning, and (2 ) what is the relation of the subject content 
of the given visual material to the subject that must be recognized and 
learned? 
We will again use a negative example. This time the subject matter is 
material on the Russian language proposed by one of our experts in methodol- 
ogy. In this chart the top section shows a picture of a forest. Below, under 
the picture of the forest is the text beginning with the word forest and then 
containing derivatives of this root word. The intention of this visual aid is 
again clear. It is necessary that the pupil concretely represent to himself the 
material with which he must deal in the lesson, and this is necessary to prevent 
his learning it “‘formally.”’ Let us assume that this is so. We will, however, 
analyze this visual aid from the point of view of both points indicated above. 
Does it serve to enrich the child’s imagination so that the child would 
recognize and visually present to himself what the word forest means? Let 
us assume that the child actually does not know this, and that it is necessary 
for him that this be especially clarified with a picture (although in texts and 
study aids on the Russian language for the second grade alone a forest is 
pictured in various illustrations seven times!). Then this means that the role 
of the picture is unique and does not correspond with the role that the text 
of the given visual aid fulfills and for which it was generally intended: to 
give the pupil some understanding of the root of the word. Moreover, the 
process itself of relating the word to the object it represents, which is evoked 
162 
SUPPLEMENT 
in the pupil by the picture drawn, and those psychological processes that 
lead to recognizing the cominon root in words with similar roots are essential- 
ly processes that have seemingly opposite directions. 
In order to see this we will proceed to examine the visual aid from the 
point of view of the second point — that which must be recognized in it by 
the child. Evidently, what must be recognized here is specifically the com- 
monality of the root of the written word, and certainly not the commonality 
of their object meaning (after all in objective meaning the words forest and 
growth or the words blackbird and black grouse have more in common than 
the words with the common root forest and forester or blackbird and ink). 
This means conversely that it is necessary for the child to be able to withdraw 
from the object meaning of the words being compared so that the word 
would appear before him as a word and not as the thing it signifies. And this 
is a very serious question. One must remember that for preschool children a 
phenomenon that Potebnya initially expressed by the figurative term word- 
glass is typical; it consists of the fact that perceiving the word, the child rec- 
ognizes 
its object content as if directly “‘shining” through it and only gradual- 
ly begins to perceive the word as such; we as well as other authors (A. R. 
Luria) had the opportunity to observe this in detail under experimental con- 
ditions. !? 
Thus the subject of consciousness of the child in the given instance must 
be the words being compared and the commonality of their root as a language 
phenomenon; the pictures on which the child’s learning is based when this 
visual aid is used fix his consciousness, on the contrary, on the object itself, 
which is represented by the root of the word. Here a similar and unnecessary 
“tying in” of that which represents the greatest generalization in the word — 
its root — to the single concrete-object picture only confuses the matter. It 
is not surprising that when the child subsequently meets the problem of 
checking unstressed vowels or changing the word in the case of a doubtful 
consonant that he does not consider it possible to check the word storozhka 
with the word storozh or to change the word kruzhka to the word kruzhit’ 
for the same reason that he must not compare the word kolbasa with the 
word kol, for example, because they signify completely different concrete 
objects: storozhka is house, an outside structure, and storozh is a man who 
guards a collection of goods, school property, etc. 
It develops that in the given case it would be better to cut the pictures 
away from the text and to use them separately. 
*G. D, Lukov, “Children’s recognition of speech in the process of play,” Scientific Reports of the 
Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, Vol. 1, 1949, pp. 65-103. 
L,I. Bozhovich, ‘The psychology of rules for unstressed vowels in roots,” Soviet Pedagogy, Nos. 
5-6, 1937. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
163 
Inasmuch as my task is a psychological analysis of the problem of rec- 
ognizing visual material, I will limit myself to one general conclusion directly 
resulting from this analysis. 
It is that the place and role of visual material in the process of teaching is 
determined by the relationship of that activity of the learner in which the 
given material can occupy a structural place as the object of the direct goal 
of his action to the activity that leads to recognizing what he must learn. 
This relationship may be of three kinds. In the first place, the one and the 
other activity may correspond; this ensures the most direct effect of visual 
aids. Further, the first activity may prepare for the second, and then it is 
necessary only to single out correctly and precisely the corresponding stages 
of the pedagogical process; finally, the activities may not be connected with 
each other, in which case the visual material is useless and may even play a 
distracting role. 
Thus, psychological analysis leads us to raising the following pedagogical 
question: finding the concrete place of visual material, that is, finding not 
only amethod of introducing it into the pedagogical process but also a method 
of such directing of the process itself as will ensure not a formal ‘“‘application”’ 
of visual aids but a genuine use of them. Because, using the words of Pirogov, 
“neither visual aids nor words in themselves without ability to use them as 
they should be used . 
. 
. will accomplish anything.’’!* 
6 
The last question in the problem of cognition with respect to the material 
to be learned still remains. This is the most complex question. Nevertheless, 
we cannot evade it because if we do, ourrepresentation of recognizing material 
to be learned will remain one-sided and consequently false. 
From what has been said above, it follows that it is necessary to dis- 
tinguish the content that is actually recognized and the content that is only 
presented to consciousness. Making this distinction is psychologically ex- 
tremely important because it expresses the essential uniqueness of the “mech- 
anism” of consciousness itself. 
Actually, only that content is recognized which is the object of the goal- 
directed activity of the subject, that is, which occupies the structural place 
of direct goal of internal or external action in the system of one activity or 
another. This position, however, does not encompass that content which 
only “‘appears to be recognized,” that is, which is controlled by consciousness. 
In order to “‘appear to be recognized,” that is, consciously controlled, 
the given content as distinguished from that which is actually recognized 
14N_ Pirogov, Questions of Life, Works, Vol. 1, St. Petersburg, 1897, p. 116. 
164 
SUPPLEMENT 
must certainly not occupy the structural place of a goal in the activity. This 
is distinctly obvious if only from the examples given above of the recogni- 
tion of one content or another in the process of writing. After all, if it is 
necessary in order to bring about an actual recognition of the graphic side 
of writing, to make it specifically the object to which action is directed as to 
its direct result, then, on the other hand, it is capable of “appearing to be 
recognized” and, consequently, consciously controlled also in the process of 
actually composing thoughts in writing. It is far from possible, however, to 
control everything consciously. 
In this case, what kind of content may appear in this last unique form 
of consciousness — in the form of conscious control? 
We can answer this question with a case that fits perfectly. This content 
is composed of conscious operations and, correspondingly, those conditions 
to which these operations respond. 
What are operations? Literally by this term we signify a completely 
determined content of activity: Operations are those methods by which an 
action is realized. Their uniqueness is that they respond not to a motive and 
not to a goal of action but to those conditions under which the goal is as- 
signed, that is, the task (the task is the goal given under specific conditions). 
As a rule, operations, that is, methods of action, are developed socially and 
sometimes are formulated in the material, means, and tools of action. Thus, 
for example, known accounting operations are crystallized in accounts; opera- 
tions of sawing, in sawing, etc. For this reason the majority of operations in 
man’s activity are the result of training and on mastering socially developed 
methods and means of action. 
Not all operations, however, are conscious operations. Only such amethod 
of action as was formed by transforming a formerly conscious action into a 
goal-directed action is called a conscious operation. But operations exist that 
have another source, another genesis; these are operations that arise by means 
of an actual “adaptation” of action to objective conditions, or by means of 
simple imitation. Operations of the latter type, and the conditions to which 
they respond, are a content that cannot be consciously controlled without 
a special effort (although, of course, it is perceived in a form that is actually 
necessary for the given action to be realized). This content may be trans- 
formed into a content capable of “appearing consciously recognized,” that 
is, consciously controlled, only if it first becomes an object of special action 
and is specially recognized. Then, having occupied a structural place in the 
conditions of action anew (and if we have in mind the process itself, then 
having again been transformed from an action to an operation), the given 
content acquires this remarkable ability. 
Thus, for example, a child practically completely masters the grammatical 
forms without having studied his native language. Children never make mis- 
takes of the type, “the lamp stand on tables,” that is, in their speech the 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
165 
declensions, conjugations, and agreement between forms are almost always 
completely correct. What is the process through which the child learns to do 
this, that is, to master these speech operations? Evidently through the process 
of precisely practical adaptation of his speech activity to those language con- 
ditions in which it occurs, that is, in the process of ‘‘adaptation” and imita- 
tion. In this way the appropriate grammatical forms that the child uses so 
completely properly as a means of oral communication or expression cannot, 
however, be consciously controlled; for this they would first have to be a 
special object of the relations of the child — a subject of his goal-directed 
activity; otherwise they may continue to exist for him only in the form of 
“feeling for language” (L. I. Bozhovich). For this reason it is necessary to 
teach the child grammar, to teach him something he already knows practical- 
ly, and this must be done not only for orthography, since even orthography 
can be mastered only formally as sometimes actually happens (a correct 
“clerical” writing with rare, but serious, ‘“‘uncouth” errors and clichés). 
We observed this dependence between how an operation was formed and 
the consciousness of the operation itself and, correspondingly, those condi- 
tions to which it responds in experimental investigations of motor habits, 
that is, fixed motor operations. ‘5 
In this investigation completely identical habits were formed (a series of 
movements on a key apparatus). The first two of these habits, however, were 
formed within the action whose purpose was for the subject of the experi- 
ment to extinguish lights flaring up above him by pressing on the keys as 
rapidly as possible; two of the other habits were formed from the beginning 
as actions, the purpose of which the subject of the experiment saw as carrying 
out movements in a determined sequence by guiding on the lamps. Thus from 
the purely external aspect the process of forming habits in both cases took 
place in a completely similar manner: Objectively it was the same task, the 
same external conditions, the same movements, differing only in different 
series in their sequence (for example, in one series: the fourth, the sixth, the 
fifth, the second, the third, the first, the fourth keys, and so forth, and in 
the other series, the sixth, the third, the second, the fourth, the first, the fifth, 
the sixth, etc.). The difference between them was only that the structural 
place that was occupied by the sequence of movements in the activity being 
formed and fixed was not the same. In the first case this sequence embodied 
the simple condition of carrying out the action to which it was practically 
adapted ; in the second case this sequence at first appeared as something toward 
which actually the action of the subject of the experiment was directed, that 
is, it appeared for the subject as a conscious goal and only afterwards was 
transformed for him into a means of carrying out the goal requirements of 
18¥V_ I, Asnin, “Uniqueness of motor habits depending on conditions of their formation,” Scientific 
Reports of the Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, Vol. 1, 1936, pp. 37-65. 
166 
SUPPLEMENT 
the instruction: Is it possible to carry out the assigned sequence of actions 
more quickly, precisely, and confidently? 
The most important result obtained in this investigation was that under 
conditions when the given operation was formed and fixed only “‘in the 
course of action,” by means of simple motor adaptation, the subjects were 
not able to give an account of the sequence of keys in the critical experi- 
ment (or, correspondingly, of the sequence of their movements), which they 
had actually fully mastered and which they had just actually carried out. And 
conversely, when the required motor operation was structured in the form 
of an action and only afterwards fixed in the form of a lasting “‘automatic”’ 
habit the sequence of keys and movements always could be consciously 
controlled by all of the subjects. 
In the experiments conducted, which were somewhat crude but still 
quite precise in their results, the objective features of the operations, which 
were different in their genesis, appeared very definitely. Those not capable 
of being consciously controlled naturally were inadequately controlled, some- 
what rigid and inflexible. The others, that is, those that can be controlled, 
have directly opposite features. They are more labile and it is easy to change 
them voluntarily. 
Thus in addition to the difference between the consciously controlled 
content (appearing to be recognized) and the content that is not at all ap- 
parent to consciousness, there is also an objective difference in the struc- 
tural place that the given content occupies in the activity of the subject. 
The relationship of that “‘which appears to be recognized”’ to that which 
is not recognized only carries out in itself the relationship of those operations 
that originate in the form of action and those operations that are the product 
of unconscious adaptation. 
That which can “‘appear”’ in consciousness to be controlled is that content 
which formerly belonged to an action, a conscious process par excellence; it 
is the content that formerly was actually consciously recognized. In neuro- 
logical terms, operations of this type are the result of a subsequent transfer 
of the process that was initially structured at a higher level to a lower-lying 
level: Operations of the second type, however, were from the start structured 
at these lower-lying, “performing” levels. For this reason only the first dis- 
play a unique internal dynamics consisting of their being “‘pulled up” to the 
higher levels, or their being lowered again to lower-lying levels, which Bianci 
labels with the expressive term retombement. This neurophysiological dy- 
namics is evident also in that unique phenomenon that I tried to express lit- 
erally as the phenomenon of “appearing” in consciousness in the course of 
actual recognition of the direct object of action. 
The dynamics described represent the true ‘‘mechanism” of the so-called 
secondary, voluntary attention (Titchener). The disclosure of this mech- 
anism, however, changes our usual understanding of this concept. The main 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
167 
thing is that it makes it possible to approach logically, but in a different way, 
the formation and development of that type of attention which fulfills an 
important function, as if a “controlling point,” of consciousness. Evidently, 
the task here is not to make interesting a content formerly uninteresting and 
requiring a special “intensification of attention,” so that attention to it, 
while remaining voluntary, does not at the same time require “‘intensifica- 
tion”’; neither does it consist in the training (how?) of a new general capacity 
for involuntary attention, which has at the same time certain features of the 
voluntary. The real problem here is much broader and may be expressed much 
more precisely: To make it possible for one content or another to be con- 
sciously controlled under conditions when another content is actually rec- 
ognized, it is necessary that it should first occupy a structural place in the 
direct goal of action. If it subsequently enters into the composition of activity 
as one of the operations that realizes it, then “‘attention will turn” also to this 
content as if involuntarily, as if only in the course of control, but in spite of 
this, not because of those circumstances that direct the initial involuntary at- 
tention, the primitive orienting reactions. 
Here is the simplest example. 
Let us suppose that a pupil in writing bends the tails of letters such as d 
or y back in an exaggerated fashion; calling his attention to this once may 
not be sufficient; when he writes dictation again, for example, it may again 
“escape his attention”’ that the tails of these letters are exaggerated; he may 
not be able to take this into account. It is another matter if he carriers out 
a certain number of special exercises in which the subject of his action and, 
consequently, the subject of his actual consciousness will specifically be the 
proper writing of the given letters. Then entering once again into the struc- 
ture of a purposeful process of writing, it will be under control, directed. To 
the degree that in the given example we have an instance of an operation fixed 
in habit, drill is specifically required, that is, a special action that must be 
repeated — and the greater the number of times, the firmer the fixation. In 
those cases when the operation to be stopped is not fixed, numerous repeti- 
tions, of course, are not necesary. 
The relations under consideration between the actual recognized content, 
the content only controlled, and the content, which although it is perceived 
yet does not enter into the circle of the recognized content, make it possible 
to define more precisely one of the most important requirements coming out 
of the principle of the consciousness of learning. Here I have in mind the 
requirement of the consciousness of the result itself, learning. 
If we approach this requirement without having preliminarily disclosed it 
psychologically, it will appear to be extremely inconsistent. After all, a great 
number of skills and much knowledge that the pupil masters in school must 
prepare him and serve him, but it is not necessary that they should remain 
always actively recognized; they must not clutter up his consciousness. It is 
168 
SUPPLEMENT 
not said in vain that a person should not be considered literate because he 
can write grammatically, but only if he cannot write ungrammatically with- 
out thinking about it. I know a number of rules, but when I use them they 
do not occupy my consciousness, and I almost never use them with complete 
conscious awareness. Otherwise it would be totally impossible to write com- 
positions, to resolve difficult problems, to pilot a plane, or even to judge 
logically, that is, to judge being guided by logical rules. Nonetheless, they are 
completely right who consider that the requirements of conscious recogni- 
tion must be extended to the results of all study unconditionally and without 
exception. 
The inconsistency of this requirement does not at all require eclectic 
conclusions: We saw that it is resolved in those dynamic relations that join 
between them the actually recognized and the consciously controlled, that 
is, only ‘‘appearing to be recognized.” 
Arithmetic, for example, must be taught so that arithmetical facts must, 
of course, be in the consciousness, but this means that they must not simply 
fill consciousness but only occupy in it “an appropriate place at an appropriate 
time.’’ And this refers to everything we teach the pupil in school — from the 
movements of physical culture to the laws of physics and logic. 
We have noted the way and its psychological generalization of responding 
to this requirement, but the development of a concrete pedagogical method 
is a problem that is no longer psychological but didactic. 
I 
Above we have considered the recognition of learning material from the 
aspect of what is being recognized, what is the subject of consciousness. 
Now before us stands the problem that is incomparably more important: 
to consider recognition from the aspect of how material to be learned is 
recognized, what it is for the personality of the child. 
This question from the very start appeared to us as genuinely central to 
the problem of cognition. In order to place it on a concrete psychological 
footing, however, it is necessary also to introduce certain concepts that 
must be specially disclosed in advance. 
This is made necessary by the circumstance that traditional, scientific 
psychology generally did not undertake the investigation of consciousness 
as a reflection of the world depending on the subject’s life relationships 
that actually made it up, on his real life. In other words, consciousness ap- 
peared for this psychology as a unique psychological production only of the 
cognitive activity of man and not of his whole life, that is, it appeared 
intellectualistically — as knowledge and not as relations. 
But psychologically what does consciousness as relation mean? The con- 
cept of relation is somewhat general and for this reason the question about 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
169 
consciousness as about relation is posed in psychology as a question about 
the meaning for man of that which he experiences. 
The concept of meaning is also one of the major concepts that is subject 
to a preliminary disclosure in order to introduce into it a complete definabili- 
ty and to free it from the idealistic veil that usually shrouds it. For this 
reason we will move away somewhat from the direct theme of the present 
discussion, but unless we do that, it will not be possible to resolve the prob- 
lem confronting us. 
In the usual use of the word, the concept sense frequently is not dis- 
tinguished from the concept meaning. For example, we speak of the sense 
of the word or its meaning, understanding in both cases one and the same 
thing. The concept of meaning, however, does not express all the riches of 
the psychological content we find in recognizing the phenomena of objective 
activity that we “signified.” 
Meaning is that generalization of activity that is crystallized, fixed in that 
sensitive carrier of it, usually in words or word combinations. This is an 
ideal, mental form of crystallization of social experience, the social practice 
of humanity. The circle of ideas of a given society, its science, its very 
language, all of this is the essence of the system of meaning. Thus, meaning 
belongs first of all to the world of objective—historical ideal phenomena. It 
is necessary to proceed from this. 
But meaning exists also as a fact of individual consciousness. Man per- 
ceives, thinks the world to be a social—historical essence; he is equipped and 
at the same time limited by notions, the knowledge of his epoch, his society. 
The riches of his consciousness are in no case reduced to the riches of his 
personal experience. Man recognized the world not like Robinson Crusoe making 
independent discoveries on an uninhabited island. Man in the course of his 
life assimilates the experience of humanity, the experience of preceding 
generations of people, and this takes place especially in the form of his 
mastering meaning and to the degree of this mastery. Thus meaning is that 
form in which anindividual man masters what is generalized and reflected in 
human experience. 
Meaning as a fact of the individual condition does not, however, lose its 
objective content and does not become a thing that is purely “psychological.” 
Of course, that which I think, understand, or know about a triangle may 
not coincide precisely with the meaning of “triangle” assumed in geometry. 
But this is not a difference in principle. Meanings do not have existence 
except as in the consciousness of concrete people. There is no independent 
kingdom of meanings, there is no platonic world of ideas. Consequently we 
must not contrast the “geometric,” logical, generally objective meaning 
with the same meaning in the consciousness of man as a special “‘psychologi- 
cal” meaning; the difference here is not a difference between the logical and 
the psychological but rather between the general and the individual. A 
170 
SUPPLEMENT 
concept does not stop being a concept as soon as it becomes my concept; 
is it possible really for “nobody’s concept”’ to exist? This is the same kind of 
an abstraction as the biblical presentation about the Word, which “was in 
the beginning.” 
The main psychological question of meaning is a question of what is the 
real place and role of meaning in the psychic life of man, what is it in his 
life. 
In meaning, reality is disclosed to man, but in a special form. Meaning 
mediates man’s knowing the world since he knows the world as a social 
thing, that is, since his reflection of the world depends on social practice 
and includes this in itself. 
A sheet of paper is reflected in my consciousness not only as something 
rectangular, white, and covered with lines, and not only as some kind of 
structure or whole form. It is reflected in my consciousness particularly as 
a sheet of paper, as paper. Sensory impressions obtained by me from a sheet 
of paper in a determined form are interpreted in my consciousness because 
I know the corresponding meaning; otherwise a sheet of paper would remain 
for me only as something white, rectangular, etc. However — and this is very 
important in principle — when I perceive paper, I perceive this real paper and 
not the meaning “‘paper.” Introspectively, meaning is absent from my con- 
sciousness: interpreting what is perceived or what is thought in itself is not 
perceived or thought. This is a fundamental psychological fact. 
For this reason, meaning can, of course, be recognized, but only when 
the object of consciousness is not what is signified, but meaning itself, for 
example, in the study of language. Thus, psychologically meaning is that 
generalized reflection of reality developed by humanity and fixed in the 
form of language, meaning, conception, knowledge, or even in the form of 
fixed skills such as a generalized “form of action,” technical standards, etc. 
Meaning represents a reflection of reality independent of the individual 
relation to it of each man; man finds an already ready, historically complex 
system of meanings and masters it in the same way that he masters imple- 
ments or the material carrier of meaning. A real psychological fact — a fact 
of my life — is that which I master with the given meaning, to what extent 
I master it, and what it becomes for me, for my personality. On what does 
the latter depend? It depends on what kind of sense the given meaning had 
for me. 
In foreign psychology the concept of sense was developed in very dif- 
ferent directions. Muller called sense a rudimentary image; Binet — most 
perspicaciously — called it embryonic action; Vander Veldt tried to show 
experimentally the formation of sense as a result of the fact that a signal that 
was earlier indifferent to the subject acquires content conditionally connected 
with it. The majority of contemporary authors, however, take a different 
direction, considering the concept of sense only in connection with language. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
171 
Polan defines sense as the totality of all psychic phenomena evoked in 
consciousness by a word; Titchener, as a complex contextual meaning; and 
Bartlett, more specifically, as meaning that is established by the “‘wholeness”’ 
of the situation; and very many authors, as a concretization of meaning as 
a result or product of the process of “signification.” 
Thus these psychological views considered sense somehow as that which is 
created in individual consciousness by meaning. But meaning belongs to that 
circle of ideal phenomena, the phenomena of social consciousness. It develops 
that both sense and meaning are determined by consciousness, but not by 
social consciousness alone. For this reason the concept of sense introduced 
into psychology with such an interpretation leads to the separation of the 
individual consciousness of man from his real life. 
In principle the concept of sense appears to be something else if con- 
sciousness is approached from the standpoint of an analysis of life itself, 
an analysis of relations that characterize the interaction of the real subject 
with the real world that surrounds him. 
With this approach sense appears in the consciousness of man as that 
which directly reflects and carries in itself his particular life relations. 
Concretely—psychologically conscious sense is created by the objective 
relations reflected in the head of man of that which stimulates his activity, 
toward what his action is directed as its immediate result. In other words, 
sense expresses the relation of motive of activity to the immediate goal of 
action. It is necessary only to emphasize that motive must be understood 
not as an experiencing of need itself but as that objective in which this need 
finds itself in given circumstances, which makes it an object and for that 
reason directs activity to a determined result. 
Sense is always the sense of something. It does not exist as “‘pure”’ sense. 
For this reason subjectively sense belongs, as it were, to the experienced 
content itself, which seems to enter into meaning. This, incidentally, leads 
to a major misunderstanding in psychology and in psychologizing linguistics, 
which was expressed either in a complete lack of distinction of these con- 
cepts or in the fact that sense was considered as concrete meaning, depending 
on the context of the situation. Actually, although sense and meaning intros- 
pectively seem to merge in consciousness, they still have a different origin 
and change according to different laws.'© They are internally connected one 
with the other, but only in a relation that is the reverse of the above in- 
dicated: Rather, sense is concretized in meanings (as a motive, in goals) and 
not meaning in sense. 
Sense by no means is contained potentially in meaning and cannot appear 
in consciousness from meaning. Sense is engendered not by meaning but by 
life. 
16 Tt is not possible for me to consider this important question in greater detail here; for the same 
reason I must put aside also the question of the actual physiological mechanism of sense formation. 
3 
SUPPLEMENT 
In certain situations a nonconformity of sense and meaning in conscious- 
ness appears particularly clearly. For example, very deeply and completely 
and from all aspects I understand what death is, I understand its inevitability 
for man and am completely convinced of its inevitability for myself personal- 
ly; I can finally know in detail the biological nature of this process. In other 
words, I can fully master the appropriate knowledge, the meaning. How dif- 
ferent, however, can this meaning appear for me! In relation to myself the 
understanding of the inevitability of death may have no sense for me: It 
does not enter into my life, nor does it change it in any way really — not 
by one iota, not for one instant. And at the beginning of his life man usually 
conducts himself as if life would continue for a whole eternity. But if some- 
thing changes in his life, or perhaps if his life approaches its end, the same 
man considers the years left to him or even the months and hurries to bring 
to an end the fulfillment of some of his intentions and rejects others. It might 
be said that his consciousness of death has become different. Has the very 
concept, the meaning of death changed, however, or has his knowledge of it 
increased, or has it become different in his consciousness? No. Its sense for 
man has changed. 
In this example, also clear is the difference of sense from the emotional 
coloring of experiencing the meaning, from its subjective background. Also, 
in the first case, the representation of death may conversely not evoke any 
kind of strong emotional experience. 
!” 
A clear distinction between sense and meaning is particularly important 
for psychology because their relation does not remain unchangeable but 
changes in the course of historical development, forming different formations 
of consciousness and different types of its structure.'§ 
Consciousness as a relation to the world is disclosed psychologically be- 
fore us especially as a system of sense and the features of its structure, as the 
features of relations of sense to meaning. The development of sense is a 
product of development of motives of activity; the development, however, 
of motives of activity themselves is determined by the development of real 
relations of man to the world, conditioned by the objective—historical con- 
ditions of his life. Consciousness as relation — this is the sense that reality 
has for man, the sense that is reflected in his consciousness. Consequently, 
‘It should be noted, by the way, that distinguishing the sense sphere and the sphere of meaning in 
consciousness is borne out also by contemporary pathopsychological data. Thus it may be con- 
sidered as established that while a disease of occipital—parietal systems of the cortex of the brain 
leads to a disintegration of meanings and corresponding intellectual operations, a disease of the 
anterior frontal (prefrontal) systems is connected as if with the sense disturbance of the personality 
of the patient. Thus these two different spheres are represented by completely different cortical 
structures. 
*®A.N. Leont’ev, A Description of the Development of the Psyche, Moscow, 1946. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
173 
consciousness of knowledge is characterized specifically by what kind of 
sense the knowledge acquires for man. 
8 
Thus, that which I actually recognize, how I recognize it, what kind of 
sense what I recognize has for me is determined by the motive of activity in 
which my specific action is included. For this reason the question of sense 
is always a question of motive. 
Let us assume that I am reading a textbook on anatomy. Is what I am 
doing clear, is it understood? Yes and no. The goal I am pursuing is understood: 
Of course, I am reading the textbook on anatomy in order to learn anatomy. 
Understood also is the meaning of what I am doing. And yet not all of my 
action may be understood — it may not be understood specifically psycholog- 
ically. In order actually to understand it, they ask me: What is the sense of 
my studying anatomy? But to answer this question about the sense it is pos- 
sible only to indicate the motive. For this reason I say: ‘‘I need this in con- 
nection with my research.” In this way I explain what the given action is for 
me (or the whole system, the whole chain of actions), that is, what kind of 
sense it has. 
But perhaps I have told an untruth. Perhaps I am doing this because I 
want to return to the profession of a doctor and because of this I am re- 
establishing my medical knowledge; then my action has an entirely different 
sense because of some kind of circumstances hidden by me. 
The sense of the action changes together with a change of its motive. In 
its objective content the action may remain almost the same, but if it has 
acquired a new motive then psychologically it has already become different. 
It progresses in a different way, develops in a different way, leads to com- 
pletely different consequences subjectively, it occupies a different place in 
the life of the personality. 
Incidentally the so-called practical psychology — that psychology which 
is nonscientifically used by the investigator, the writer, and the common man 
about whom they say that he “understands people well” — is first of all a 
psychology of sense; its nonrecognized method is composed particularly of 
disclosing the sense of human actions. For this reason it is so personal, so 
concrete, and so genuinely vital. 
An analysis leading to an actual disclosure of sense cannot be limited to 
superficial observation. It is psychological analysis with all of its inherent 
difficulties. Its very first necessary distinction — the distinction of action and 
activity — requires penetrating into the internal content of the process. After 
all, from the process itself it is not evident what kind of process it is — action 
or activity. Often in order to explain this, active investigation is required: 
substantiating observation, hypothesis, effective verification. 
174 
SUPPLEMENT 
That to which the given process is directed may seem to be inducing it, 
embodying its motives; if this is so, then it is activity. But this same process 
may be induced by a completely different motive not at all coinciding with 
that to which it is directed as its result; then it is an action. And it may happen 
also that in the first case this process will express lofty feeling and in the 
second, craftiness. 
One and the same action realizing various relations, that is, entering into 
variously motivated activity, changes psychologically; it acquires a different 
sense. But this also means that what the subject actually recognizes as the 
object content of the given action he perceives differently. For this reason 
the only way for genuine concrete—psychological investigation of conscious- 
ness is the way of sense analysis — the way of analysis of motivation, in the 
development of which the development of psychic life of man is expressed 
from the subjective aspect. 
In spite of what it seems to be from the superficial point of view, this 
is a way that confirms the objectivity of its bases to a high degree inasmuch 
as this way leads to an understanding of the consciousness of man derived 
from life, from concrete being, and not from the laws of consciousness of 
surrounding people, not from knowledge. 
It is especially important here to emphasize the following. The fact is, 
of course, that overcoming the introspective positions still does not lead us 
beyond the limits of Hegelian conceptions of consciousness. For this reason 
it is not enough to formulate in a general way a simple antithesis to Hegel’s 
general theoretical conceptions, since if one accepts his position that nothing 
can exist for consciousness except as knowledge, it is logically impossible to 
understand how consciousness can “rise above itself,” that it is impossible to 
go into a concrete analysis of consciousness beyond its limits — into life, 
as is required by later materialism. The fact is that consciousness as thinking 
is the Hegelian thinking as a subject, “absolute subject, which for another can- 
not be and must not be an object; particularly and notwithstanding all efforts, 
such a subject will never find a transition to object, to being. Just exactly as 
a head cut away from the body cannot move to master a subject because it 
does not have the means, it does not have the grasping organs.””!9 
In the history of psychology a factual understanding of consciousness 
as recognition appears with a special sharpness in the problem of emotions. 
It practically made the problem of emotions psychologically hopeless, reduc- 
ing it in essence to a physiological problem, and factually leaving only a 
descriptive investigation as psychology. If we draw away from the com- 
pletely empty results of the latter (‘I would prefer,” wrote James, ““wordy 
descriptions of the New Hampshire cliffs.”), then the only psychological 
L. Feuerbach, Collected Philosophical Works, Vol. 1, Moscow, 1955, p. 200. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
175 
theory of emotional experiences is the intellectualistic theory because, as 
was completely correctly indicated by J. Dumas in his introduction to the 
French translation of James, in so-called peripheral conceptions, what is 
under consideration is comparison of the intellectualistic explanation with 
the physiological explanation, not with the psychological.2° But after all, 
the psychological explanation did not directly compare the substance of 
either one or the other psychological theory. For this reason neither 
James 
and Lange, on the one hand, nor Cannon and his co-workers, on the other, 
are in a position to overcome intellectualism in the plane of the real psy- 
chological consideration of this problem. The classical “telegram argument” 
(in order for a telegram to evoke one experience or another, it must first 
be understood) maintains all of its force in any representation about the 
physiological mechanism of emotion. Thus, even if we explain the mech- 
anism of the emotional experience itself, it will nevertheless remain, from 
the point of view of traditional teaching about consciousness, psychological- 
ly determined specifically by “‘consciousness as knowing.” 
The problem of overcoming this position cannot consequently consist 
of changing the psychological representation of the nature of experiences 
themselves, which expresses the relation of the subject to what is known; 
it cannot consist of establishing an internal connection between them, for 
that which was separated from the very beginning cannot be united later 
except externally, and, it is understood, a bare-faced declaration of its unity, 
just like any kind of generally bare declaration, does not really change any- 
thing. The real problem is to understand the consciousness of man as reflect- 
ing his real life, his existence. And for this it is necessary to reject decisively, 
in considering consciousness, the idealistic abstraction of purely cognitive 
processes — abstractions that later inevitably lead to a lifeless treatment of 
thinking itself. 
In the simple idea that if geometry contradicted our passions and our 
interests, then we would argue against it and would violate it in spite of all 
the evidence of Euclid and Archimedes is contained a great truth. It is neces- 
sary to be able to thoroughly understand this truth psychologically. 
Meanwhile, we can only repeat once more: That aspect of consciousness 
of the individual that is determined by his own life relationships is sense. In 
relation to cognitive processes sense is that which makes these processes not 
only directed but also partial, which generally imparts to thinking a psy- 
chologically cogent character distinguishing in principle the intellectual 
processes taking place in the human head from those sometimes very com- 
plex processes of accounting that are carried out by calculators. 
Let us say at once: From this it is evident that the problem of the forma- 
tion and development of thinking cannot be wholly reduced to the problem 
20W. James, Le Théorie de l’Emotion, Introduction, Paris, 1902. 
176 
SUPPLEMENT 
of mastering knowledge, mental skills, and habits. After all, relations and 
sense cannot be taught. Sense can only be disclosed in the process of studying 
incorporated into a clearly perceived and developed idea, which enriches the 
student with corresponding knowledge and skills. 
Sense is not taught — sense is nurtured. The unity of nurturing and teach- 
ing is that concrete—psychological unity for forming sense and meaning. 
Those internal cogent relations that connect nurturing and teaching appear 
from the aspect of the process of formation of consciousness specifically as 
the relation of sense and meaning. 
These relations when considered appear before us as real relations of the 
activity of man itself. Owing to this, their analysis can become for us a 
method of psychological investigation of consciousness. 
At the very outset in the investigation of the so-called visual—active think- 
ing of small children we met with the dependence of understanding on the 
sense that his actions have for the child. We were struck by the nonidentical 
data we obtained in the experiments and their frequent clear noncorrespond- 
ence with the actual intellectual capacities of the child. Thus, for example, 
certain simpler tasks structured after the type of well-known tasks of Kohler 
were sometimes not solved even by children who were six to ten years old, 
while they presented no special difficulty to younger children. This made us 
specifically isolate the problem of the method of investigation of the in- 
tellect and to make it the subject of special experimental treatment. 
Work was undertaken in this area by V. I. Asnin?! and showed that the 
success of the process of solving tasks not only is determined by its objective 
content but depends most of all on the motive that impels the child’s action, 
in other words, on the sense that this particular activity has for him. 
In order to explain the mode of activity of the child solving the task 
presented to him, the author of the paper cited used the method of the 
“paired” experiment carried out under conditions of a special observation 
room with a one-way glass window and microphone apparatus, which allowed 
the experimenter to be present at the experiment as if in a cap that made him 
invisible, that is, able to see and hear the children but to remain unseen and 
unheard by them. The “‘pairing” of the experiment consisted of having an- 
other child enter the room at the desired moment who by his comments and 
actions forced the subject of the experiment to reveal his motives. 
au V. I, Asnin, “On the question of conditions of reliability of psychological investigation of the 
intellect,” Theses of Reports of the Scientific Session of the Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, 1938; 
V. I. Asnin, “Conditions of reliability of the psychological experiment,” Scientific Notes of the 
Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, 1941, Da less 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
177 
The following is one of the simpler facts that was obtained from these 
experiments. 
The subject, a girl of school age, cannot solve the task presented to her, 
which is very simple: to obtain an object lying in the middle of the table at 
such a distance from the edge and so fenced off by a low barrier that reaching 
it directly by hand is impossible and for this reason it is necessary to use a 
small cane lying nearby. 
Into the room comes the second girl, five years old. The experimenter 
again reminds the older girl, the subject of the experiment, that she must 
somehow try to get the thing lying on the table, and he leaves. The older 
girl continues her earlier attempts; she goes from one side of the table to the 
other, but without results. The younger girl from the beginning watches her 
in silence and then proposes, “Why don’t you jump!” The older girl pays no 
attention at all to this clearly worthless advice and continues her actions 
silently. Then the younger girl gives a new piece of advice, to get the cane, 
and taking it she tries to reach the object herself. The older girl, however, 
quickly takes the cane from her and replaces it, explaining that there is no 
difficulty in getting the object with the cane, that “‘anyone could do that.” 
At this moment the experimenter comes in and the older girl explains 
to him that she cannot get the thing from the table. It is characteristic that 
in this situation many subjects behaved in the same way, and the introduc- 
tion into the task of an attractive goal (a school compass, etc.), which the 
subjects could keep if they got it, did not change the general picture but 
only made the behavior of the subjects more emotionally colored. 
The real motive impelling the subject to act evidently does not cor- 
respond here with the motive that the experimenter tries to present to him 
in promising him the object reached as a reward. Although the subject accepts 
this condition, actually he is still motivated by another motive — to show his 
cleverness, his ingenuity, etc. As a result of this the task put before him is 
overinterpreted and the solution that is objectively better and simpler has 
lost sense for him (“anybody can do that’’). 
This case, regardless of its simplicity, presents certain essential questions 
that arise in the psychological analysis of any conscious intellectual activity. 
First of all, there is the question that is very important in principle: 
Are we dealing in such cases with an inappropriate objective task and its 
understanding by the subject, or with the characteristics of its sense for 
the subject? For traditional psychology this question does not exist; its 
explanation, of course, is the first hypothesis appealing especially to the 
subject’s understanding of the task, which, it seems, determines for him its 
sense. This explanation, however, is fallacious. 
In order to demonstrate this, it is sufficient to change the conditions 
of the experiment somewhat, specifically, without decreasing the objective 
significance for the subject’s consciousness of the object he was to obtain, 
simply to explain to him that he can use the cane. The subject, of course, 
178 
SUPPLEMENT 
proceeds in this case according to instructions but tries to avoid the agreed- 
on reward. This is evident because he tries to refuse it or accepts it un- 
willingly, ‘forgets’ it afterwards on the laboratory table, etc. This phenom- 
enon sometimes appears with extraordinary force: It seems that for this, it 
is sufficient to abruptly separate the task and the result, for example, to 
make the goal-reward more significant without complicating the task. Under 
this condition it is possible to produce a real affect in the child (only in a 
child?). 
What are these and similar facts evidence of? How is an internal need to 
prove his right to obtain a reward through efforts that he could easily avoid 
produced in the child? One thing is completely obvious: The thing here is not 
understanding the problem or even understanding the situation as a whole. 
A person who is not a psychologist would simply say, this is a question not 
of intelligence but of conscience. And this probably would annoy the 
scientific psychologist because traditional psychology has nothing to do 
with such categories as conscience; traditional psychology generally does not 
deal with concepts in which ethical categories may be psychologically dis- 
closed (which, however, does not interfere with its pretensions to a psy- 
chological elucidation of questions of education!). 
The whole difficulty that these simple facts present for traditional psy- 
chological analysis is that these facts do not characterize the consciousness 
of the child from the aspect of its “function,” and they are not determined 
by those meanings that have a goal, conditions of action for him, require- 
ments of the experimenter, etc. Even less can they be explained by his 
emotions. The key to their understanding lies in the special features of the 
motives for activity, in the sphere of motives: They are determined by the 
sense of the child for a given task, a given situation. 
Psychologically the irreducibility of sense to meaning is as indisputable 
as the irreducibility of ethical categories to arithmetical. In psychology a 
similar reduction forms a theoretical basis for intellectualism, in ethics, a 
basis of that “morality based on arithmetic’’ (Herzen) that ‘“‘the bourgeois 
thought up for themselves.” 
The second general question that analysis of the facts cited above places 
before the psychological investigation of consciousness refers to the problem 
of dependence of the intellectual processes themselves on the motivation of 
activity. Both of the questions, however, appear in the facts cited in an ex- 
tremely simplified and rough form — as a problem of using or not using a 
suitable method to resolve the problem. In order that this problem might 
appear more cogent, it is necessary to make the situation more complex. For 
this reason only, further special investigations devoted to the mastery of 
meanings might be able to explain in greater detail the real dependence 
that exists here. Thus, for example, it was possible to show that only under 
conditions of the formation of strictly cognitive motives was a real and not 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
179 
just a formal mastery of operations of theoretical thought possible. Other- 
wise these operations, like the theoretical knowledge itself with which their 
formation is connected, are assimilated only formally in contrast with what 
one might expect from the usual simplified understanding of formalism in 
education.?? 
In this section we will not consider the problem of intellect. This is a 
separate problem. For our purposes it is sufficient to formulate only a gen- 
eral psychological thesis referring to the dependences considered: The 
generation of new motives forming new senses discloses new possibilities 
also in the sphere of the intellect. This thesis is very well expressed in the 
words of L. Feuerbach: “That to which the heart is open cannot remain a 
secret for the mind.” 
10 
The problem of the consciousness of learning arose for us from its psy- 
chological aspect as a problem of what kind of sense the knowledge the child 
acquires has for him. Consequently, that which this learning becomes for the 
child and how he acquires it must be determined by concrete motives impel- 
ling him to learn. It is not difficult to understand that this is truly so. 
Let us assume that the child is preparing a lesson in history because until 
he finishes preparing the lesson he will not be allowed to go to the movies; 
let us assume now that he does this not for this reason but because he wants 
to obtain a good mark; we will assume further that he is fascinated by the 
content of the textbook itself; finally, let us assume that in studying history, 
he sees a way to his future profession and this compels him to study history 
intensely. Will the results in all these cases be the same? Obviously not. The 
difference here will consist not only in the success of learning but also in the 
degree of its consciousness — what will that which is learned be for the child, 
what place will the learning occupy in his life, in his personality, what sense 
will it acquire for him? 
We will first consider the first, the more simple question, the question of 
dependence of success of study on the sense that what he has studied has for 
the child. 
This question is usually posed as a question of the role of interest in 
study. The more interesting the material to be learned is for the child, the 
more easily he assimilates it and the more easily he remembers it. The prob- 
lem of interest as a problem of attention belongs thus to the number of psy- 
chological problems that are very important for practical pedagogy. But like 
227. I. Bozhovich, “The psychological nature of formalism in school learning,” Soviet Pedagogy, No. 
9, 1945. 
180 
SUPPLEMENT 
the problem of attention, it requires further analysis because like attention, 
interest is no more than a phenomenon whose essence and basis must still 
be found. The task is as follows: If success depends on interest, then in that 
case what determines interest itself? 
Interests are frequently connected with emotions, with needs; sometimes 
attempts are made to find a dependence of interests on thought, but most 
often there is only a criticism of ‘“‘one-sided”’ theory without, however, any 
kind of positive resolution of the problem.”? This is natural because even the 
pedagogy of interest is limited by inadequately analyzed recommendations, 
the rightness of which is easily demonstrated in the practice of masters of 
pedagogy, but teaching others to use these is extremely difficult. This cir- 
cumstance is well known and there is no need to cite examples for it. The 
point here is that those relations within the very activity of the child that 
lie behind the appearance of interest and through which alone it is possible 
really to control still remain hidden. 
In order to see these relations it would be necessary to find and investigate 
the methods themselves of directing interests and this under conditions of 
their most free expression. Work with children in institutions outside school 
presents such conditions to the highest degree. In two broad, collective ex- 
perimental investigations that we have undertaken, one of which was carried 
out in the Kharkov Pioneer Palace (1933-1934) and the other in Moscow’s 
Gorky Park (1935), we set before ourselves the task, based on a theoretical 
analysis of this structure of activity, of exercising control over and directing 
children to one goal or another in which interest was objectively expressed. 
First of all, we had the possibility of once more confirming, as was al- 
ready experimentally demonstrated by Simons and Chaze,”* that conscious- 
ness alone of the objective meaning content of one object or another was 
still inadequate for the arousal of interest (in the psychological meaning of 
this term), and that, conversely, interest easily becomes a means of changing 
the structure of activity, particularly the means of changing its motive. 
We will cite certain facts from this research. 
A recognized inadequacy of the work of the aviation circle of the Pioneer 
Palace was that its youngest participators, working with great fascination on 
the preparation of model airplanes, did not, however, show sufficient interest 
in theoretical information necessary for conscientious construction. It ap- 
peared actually that doing the painstaking work of bending the ribs, etc., 
** It is characteristic that S. A. Anan’in in his well-known review comes to the conclusion that “‘the 
condition of interest as such simply does not exist” (see his book, Interest according to the Teaching 
of Modern Psychology and Pedagogy, Moscow, 1915). Thus the problem of interest shares even in 
this respect the fate of the problem of attention, which, like interest, is either transformed into the 
main explanatory principle or completely denied. 
24P. Simons and D. Chaze, ‘‘Practice vs. Motivation,” Journal of Educational Research, No. 1, 1929. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
181 
willingly and skillfully, the groups of beginning model plane builders were 
very little interested in the theory of flight; many could not properly answer 
the questions about why an airplane remains in the air, what “frontal re- 
sistance”’ is, what “‘angle of attack” is, why the model plane may fall before 
its motor stops running, etc. No propaganda for the necessity of understand- 
ing the theoretical aspect of the matter had any success and even in reading 
popular literature on aviation, the children ‘‘read out of it”’ for themselves 
almost exclusively technical information that had a practical character. 
The problem was to elicit in the young club members an active interest 
in the physical bases of flight. After certain trial attempts the work of the 
experimental group was restructured in the following way. In place of the 
usual task — to produce the best model — another task was placed before 
the children: to “fly” the model built for the purpose a determined distance 
along a straight line as soon as possible. All members of the circle built 
models (some of the components came as semifinished parts to save time 
for the subsequent activity), and then on appointed days all were given an 
equal opportunity to carry out “‘test”’ starts, the results of which were sub- 
sequently posted on the board. Thus, after the models were assembled, the 
first start was organized, which of course had different results for the dif- 
ferent young builders, judged by the distance over which the model flew. 
Then after a break intended for correcting and improving the models, the 
start was repeated and its results added to the first results, etc., until some- 
body’s model did ‘“‘fly”’ the preliminarily established total distance. 
It is clearly understandable that through this experimental reconstruc- 
tion of the activity of the club members, there occurred a very great change 
in the interest. The new task was accepted by the children as willingly, it is 
understood, as was the former task; as distinct from the first task, however, 
in impelling activity (that is, as a motive) it contained what was needed in 
setting goals that were objectively theoretical, cognitive. Why does the 
model ascend steeply and then, without having flown two meters, fall 
precipitously? What must be changed in it for the next flight? This must be 
analyzed. It seems that the angle of attack must be smaller. On the board 
the instructor draws vector arrows — ahead, up, down — one set of arrows 
grows, another decreases; it is clear under these conditions that the airplane 
inevitably will fall. This is very interesting. And now when the hand of the 
young builder smooths the surface of the plane, he has in his head the rela- 
tionship of these arrow-vectors. 
How sharp an effect can be achieved in creating interest by changing the 
structure of activity is particularly clearly obvious from the materials of an 
investigation carried out in the children’s area in the park. As the result of 
changes in the work of a flight laboratory similar to those just mentioned, 
the following data were obtained: The average number of children involved 
in work per day (average for 12 days) increased from 6.6 to 40.7. 
182 
SUPPLEMENT 
Similarly distinct data were obtained by G. L. Rozengart in the park’s 
Home of the Young Technician as a result of an experiment with many 
hundreds of children visitors. The task was twofold: to maintain interest 
for a longer time in the general cycle, ““Magnetism,” in the electromechan- 
ical laboratory and to compel interest in posters giving the theoretical ex- 
planations which usually are not read by children. The quantitative results 
were as follows. Before reconstructing the character of the work approxi- 
mately 60% of the children maintained attention on the objects of the cycle 
for less than 3 minutes, 30% from 3 to 5 minutes, and 10% for more than 5 
minutes. After reconstruction (but without changing the objects themselves) 
the number of children whose attention was maintained on the cycle for less 
than 3 minutes was 0%; from 3 to 5 minutes, 5%; from 5 to 10 minutes, 
15%; from 10 to 20 minutes, 50%; and more than 20 minutes, 30%. It is 
remarkable that now whole groups of children collected around the posters. 
The persuasiveness of these data is great because the conditions provided 
for the work completely excluded the role of any kind of extraneous factors; 
the children came freely and left the pavilion freely; nothing could keep 
them except interest in the cycle being demonstrated. This also accounts for 
the very remarkable number (60%) of short visits, less than 3 minutes, to the 
laboratory by children before the experimental reconstruction of the work; 
on the other hand, the sharp increase in time indices achieved by the re- 
construction is most significant. 
The theoretical result of these investigations is just as clear as their factual 
results. The emerging motive creates purpose for action, this is understood. 
However, the specific type of motives, as for example, cognitive motives, 
assumes very complex systems of many actions, and this also means seeking 
and interpreting many goals that of course were not present earlier. For this 
reason the general direction created by this type of motive is much broader 
than the direction of action taken alone or of an isolated goal. This wide 
circle of aims is also the circle of the given interest. Consequently, to make 
something interesting means (1) to make it active or to reestablish a given 
motive and (2) to make the corresponding goals desirable. In other words, 
in order to arouse interest it is necessary not to indicate the goal and then 
try to motivationally justify the action and the direction of the given goal, 
but it is necessary, on the contrary, to create a motive and then to disclose 
the possibility of reaching the goal (usually a whole system of intermediate 
and “‘indirect”’ goals) in one or another subject content. Thus activity that 
arouses interest is activity that is accomplished by only a more or less cir- 
cumscribed sphere of action; an interesting school subject is that school 
subject which has come within the ‘“‘sphere of goals” of the learner in con- 
nection with one or another motive that moves him. Under this condition 
the substance of the content of the given subject occupies the structural 
place of goal in the learning activity of the learner; consequently, it be- 
comes actually recognized by him and easily remembered (P. I. Zinchenko). 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
183 
But the given school subject may, of course, interest the pupil variously. 
In essence, as a subject of his actual consciousness, its content may appear 
for him somewhat different, and this depends on the motive informing the 
sense of studying the given school subject. For this reason an investigation of 
interests cannot limit itself to disclosing only the structural, formal relations 
of activity and inevitably requires penetration into the motivational sphere 
determining interests qualitatively, from their internal sense aspect. 
11 
Thus everything takes us to the same simple idea, to the idea of depen- 
dence of cognitive content of consciousness on the relation to what is know- 
able. This is an old, it may be said, classical, idea for pedagogy. And the 
problem for psychology consists not of ‘‘substantiating”’ it but of disclosing 
it concretely—psychologically. 
What has already been found allows us to approach this idea in one of 
its most important points in a different way, in the question of ways of 
forming consciousness and consciousness as a relation. 
The requirement arising from the principle of conscious study includes 
in itself the requirement of clear understanding by the child of why and for 
what purpose he must study. It is necessary that the child should understand 
that one must study in order to become a valuable member of society, its 
worthy builder, defender of one’s country, etc., that to study is the duty 
of a child. This is indisputable; this is categorically so. 
Nonetheless, such a requirement of consciousness is still abstract. 
It is abstract because it reduces the whole question to a question of 
understanding and knowledge by the child of why it is necessary to study. 
Actually, however, understanding this is only a prerequisite, a condition for 
the consciousness of learning. 
Is it possible to explain to the child why it is necessary to study? Of 
course it is. And this can and should be done as completely as possible 
and in sufficient detail. After all, even a young school child is capable of 
understanding and capable of speaking reasonably and convincingly about 
this himself. 
The point, however, is that neither understanding nor knowledge of the 
meaning of what is studied characterizes consciousness; consciousness as a 
relation is the sense that what is learned acquires for the child. Not making 
the distinction, confusing the one with the other, is not only psychologically 
false but also engenders “‘intellectualistic” formalism in practice. 
A first or second grader knows why he studies, he knows why in general 
it is necessary to study. But does this really compel him to listen attentively 
to the teacher and carefully do his homework assignment? No, this is not so. 
In reality other motives lead him to study: Perhaps he simply wants to learn 
184 
SUPPLEMENT 
to read, to write, and to count; perhaps he wants to get high marks; perhaps 
he wants to maintain his reputation in the family, in the class, in the eyes of 
the teacher. 
What determines the sense that what is learned has for the child is what 
he knows about the necessity for studying or the real motives for study. 
According to our basic position, the relation of the direct object of action to 
the motive of the activity in which it is included is what we call sense. This 
means that the sense that the object of his learning activity acquires for the 
child, the object of his study, is determined by the motives of his learning 
activity. This sense characterizes the consciousness of his assimilating know- 
ledge. This means that it is insufficient for a child to learn the meaning of 
a given subject whether theoretical or practical, but it is necessary that he 
should have a proper attitude toward what he is studying; it is necessary 
to nurture in him the required relation. Only under these conditions will 
the knowledge he has acquired be living knowledge for him, will it become 
“an organ of his individuality,” and in its turn, determine his relation to 
the world. 
If we take the question of consciousness in its more general aspect, then 
it must not be posed as, for example, “‘is the child capable of understanding 
what the fatherland is?”’ but in such a way as Dobrolyubov posed it, can the 
child “‘fit fatherland into himself?’’ Behind the external difference in the 
words there hides an internal difference in consciousness. “To fit into one- 
self,’ this is not the same thing as “‘to understand.” After all, what is it, to 
understand, and on the basis of what do we usually judge understanding? 
On the basis of the student’s ability to explain, speak about, write a com- 
position on a given theme. But this ability is not yet evidence of the fact 
that what he has been told has become for the pupil internally his, that it 
“lodged”’ in his personality. 
A. S. Makarenko justifiably required for this reason not being satisfied 
with words but confirming what is hidden behind them. ‘‘A schoolboy says 
that frontier guards must be bold and that he also wants to be bold, and 
thinks that it is necessary to be bold. Did you ascertain whether he is bold 
or cowardly?”’*5 
I have now used as an illustration the question of what can such con- 
cepts as fatherland or boldness become for the child. This question refers, 
however, rather to the area of education and perhaps with respect to train- 
ing and acquiring knowledge (for example, in mathematics or in physics); 
the problem of sense generally does not occur and here must we speak only 
of meaning or of mastering knowledge? This is a grossly inaccurate idea. In 
mathematics and in physics it is possible to acquire knowledge in such a 
** Scientific Reports of the Kharkov Pedagogical Institute, Vol. 6, 1941, p. 5. 
PSYCHOLOGICAL QUESTIONS ON CONSCIOUSNESS OF LEARNING 
185 
way that it will be dead and will remain dead until such a time as perhaps 
life itself resurrects it, if it did not finally erode from the memory of the 
student by that time. After all, one of the pupils of the seventh grade admitted 
that he had not once been confronted by such a “‘notable occasion”’ as 
would allow him to use the knowledge he had acquired in physics!?¢ 
Of course, in mastering school subjects (just as in mastering any kind of 
knowledge in general, as in mastering science), it is decisively important 
what kind of place cognition occupies in the life of man, whether it is a part 
of real life for him or only external, a condition coupled to it externally. 
“Science,” wrote Herzen, ‘‘must be lived through in order that it not be 
acquired formally.”?” And in training also in order not to assimilate material 
formally, it is necessary not to “‘serve’’ a teaching period but to live it, it is 
necessary that learning should enter into life, that it should have a vital sense 
for the learner. 
Even in training habits, ordinary motor habits, this is also so. Even the 
methods of bayonet fighting cannot be mastered if there is no internal rela- 
tion as a motive for this and if the whole thing looks like bare technique 
of “‘long stabs” and “‘short stabs,” ‘‘repelling up” and “‘‘repelling down.” 
Even here the old classical ‘““Be angry!” which from time immemorial was 
required by commanders of the Russian soldier is desirable. 
Intellectualistic understanding of consciousness is not only abstract, but, 
as I have already said, it is deeply metaphysical because it is devoid of the 
idea of development. 
It gives no perspective, it introduces into the relation of consciousness 
no system of subsequent concrete, educational tasks; the requirements 
emanating from it remain the same, whether we speak of pupils of the first 
or the tenth grade. After all, a child may learn very consciously even in the 
first grade, he may remain a very conscious student even in the higher 
classes in the school, and finally he may become a conscious student in a 
higher educational institution; and the consciousness of his learning will 
vary at all of these stages. This is self-evident. Consequently the concrete 
task of nurturing a conscious relation to learning, to what is learned, is 
different for various steps of development with respect to children of various 
ages. To give a perspective to the education of consciousness means to rec- 
ognize these problems. And this requires that they be expressed not in 
terms of a final result but in terms of development. Now we know that they 
must be presented as problems of development, education, of the motives 
of studying. We know still another thing: that motives are formed in the 
life activity of the child; to the uniqueness of life corresponds the unique- 
26 TI, Bozhovich, “Psychological nature of formalism of school subjects,” Soviet Pedagogy, No. 9, 
1945. 
274 P. Herzen, Collected Works in Thirty Volumes, Vol. 3, Moscow, 1954, p. 68. 
186 
SUPPLEMENT 
ness of the motivational sphere of the personality; for this reason motives 
cannot be developed along isolated lines unconnected one with another. 
Consequently, we must speak of the problems of nurturing the motives 
for learning in connection with the development of life, with the develop- 
ment of the content of actual vital relations of the child; only under these 
conditions will the tasks that have been introduced be sufficiently concrete 
and real, which is the main thing. 
_ 
Thus training and acquired knowledge educate, and this must not be 
undervalued. But in order that knowledge should educate, it is necessary to 
nurture the relation of knowledge itself. In this is the essence of the con- 
sciousness of learning. 
Eminent because of his psychological penetration, a Russian man of the 
eighteenth century, Grigorii Vinskii, having spent many years of his ill- 
starred life as a teacher in private homes, bitterly noted that in Russia at 
that time “‘teaching is almost universally accepted as education.” Further 
he said, “‘All fathers, all mothers, and all of you on whom children depend, 
undertake a more detailed examination of the difference between education 
and teaching; be careful to educate your children first, and then to teach 
them.” And finally he wrote, with true penetration, ‘‘Education alone is 
the distinguishing property of man; teaching, however, is not altogether 
foreign even to other creatures.’’?8 
In these words is incorporated a very important and a very profound 
idea. 
8G. S. Vinskii, My Time, Notes, St. Petersburg, 1914, pp. 9, 18-19.
